<EOS>
THE

<EOS>
Two
of
the

London
's
nightworkers
were

Street

soon

,
2nd

.
 
<EOS>
The

not

.
<EOS>
That

<EOS>
Only

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
"
 
<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
Something

<EOS>
"

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

reports
now
published
in
special

of
all

Sunday

,
and

.
 
<EOS>
Boys

,

.
 
<EOS>
Around

were

.
<EOS>
Was

,

was

?
<EOS>
Half

.
<EOS>
Yet

,

the
north

,

to

,
and

.
 
<EOS>
In

,

,

;
but

.
 
<EOS>
It

half
-

.
<EOS>
The

had
already

on

ten

,
yet
London
was
in

of
where

had
actually

,
and
utterly

.
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
II
.
 
<EOS>
EFFECT
IN
THE
CITY
.
  
<EOS>
Monday
,
3rd
September
,
1910
,
was
indeed
Black

for

.
 
<EOS>
By
midnight
on
Sunday
the
appalling

had

everywhere
.
<EOS>
Though

,
yet

that

had
been

in
the

,

.
 
<EOS>
Before

,

the

of

we

,
while
all
unconscious
,

.
 
<EOS>
With

,

<EOS>
It

.
<EOS>
And

had
,

and

,

of
the
sea
and

.
 
<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
The

of

was

.
 
<EOS>
Eager

into
the

by

the

the

,

<EOS>
indignant

.
 
<EOS>
As

banks
were
opened
there
was
a
run
on

,
but
by
noon
the
Bank
of
England
had

.
<EOS>
The

,
being
thus

to

,

,
bringing

.
<EOS>
Consols
stood
at
90
on
Saturday
,
but
by

were
down
to
42
-
-lower

than

1798
,
when

47
-
1/4
.
<EOS>
Numbers

tried

,

,
for

being

they

not

.
 
<EOS>
On
the
Stock
Exchange
the

in

was

.
<EOS>
Securities
of
every

went
entirely

,
and

.
<EOS>
Financiers
were
surprised
that
no
warning
in
London
had
betrayed
the
position
of
affairs
,
London
being
the

.
<EOS>
Prior

1870
Paris
shared

the

the
money

,
but

,

.
<EOS>
Had

that

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
Yet

,

<EOS>
Thus

<EOS>
Thus

's

war

.
<EOS>
Its

,

,

,

the

from

<EOS>
This

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
Plenty

<EOS>
Thus

<EOS>
Business

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Excited

,

<EOS>
the

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Certain

<EOS>
In

,

.
 
<EOS>
In

"
on
'

,
"

<EOS>
Liverpool

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
By

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
In

,

,

<EOS>

<EOS>
It

.
 
<EOS>
Upon

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
Germany

,

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

WE

<EOS>

<EOS>
[

:
THE
ENEMY
'S
FAMOUS

.
]
  
<EOS>
The
public
,
however
,
little
dreamed
of
the
hopeless
confusion
in
the
War
Office
,
in
the
various
regimental
depots
throughout
the
country
,
at
headquarters
everywhere
,
and
in
every
barracks
in
the
kingdom
.
<EOS>
The
armed
forces
of
England
were
passing
from
a
peace
to
a
war
footing
;
but
the
mobilisation
of
the
various
units
--
namely
,
its
completion
in
men
,
horses
,
and
material
--
was
utterly
impossible
in
the
face
of
the
extraordinary
regulations
which
,
kept
a
strict
secret
by
the
Council
of
Defence
until
this
moment
,
revealed
a
hopeless
state
of
things
.
 
<EOS>
The
disorder
was
frightful
.
<EOS>
Not
a
regiment
was
found
fully
equipped
and
ready
to
march
.
<EOS>
There
was
a
dearth
of
officers
,
equipment
,
horses
,
provisions
--
of
,
indeed
,
everything
.
<EOS>
Men
had
guns
without
ammunition
;
cavalry
and

were
without
horses
;
engineers
only
half
equipped
;
volunteers
with
no
transport
whatever
;
balloon
sections
without
balloons
,
and
searchlight
units
vainly
trying
to
obtain
the
necessary
instruments
.
 
<EOS>
Horses
were
being
requisitioned
everywhere
.
<EOS>
The
few
horses
that
,
in
the
age
of
motor
-
cars
,
now
remained
on
the
roads
in
London
were
quickly
taken
for
draught
,
and
all
horses
fit
to
ride
were
commandeered
for
the
cavalry
.
 
<EOS>
During
the
turmoil
daring
German
spies
were
actively
at
work
south
of
London
.
<EOS>
The
Southampton
line
of
the
London
and
South
-
Western
Railway
was
destroyed
--
with
explosives
placed
by
unknown
hands
--
by
the
bridge
over
the
Wey
,
near
Weybridge
,
being
blown
up
;
and
again
that
over
the
Mole
,
between
Walton
and
Esher
,
while
the
Reading
line
was
cut
by
the
great
bridge
over
the
Thames
at
Staines
being
destroyed
.
<EOS>
The
line
,
too
,
between
Guildford
and
Waterloo
,
was
also
rendered
impassable
by
the
wreck
of
the
midnight
train
,
which
was
blown
up
half
-
way
between
Wansborough
and
Guildford
,
while
in
several
other
places
nearer
London
bridges
were
rendered
unstable
by
dynamite
,
the
favourite
method
apparently
being
to
blow
the
crown
out
of
an

.
 
<EOS>
The
well
-
laid
plans
of
the
enemy
were
thus
quickly
revealed
.
<EOS>
Among
the
thousands
of
Germans
working
in
London
,
the
hundred
or
so
spies
,
all
trusted
soldiers
,
had
passed
unnoticed
but
,
working
in
unison
,
each
little
group
of
two
or
three
had
been
allotted
its
task
,
and
had
previously
thoroughly
reconnoitred
the
position
and
studied
the
most
rapid
or
effective
means
.
 
<EOS>
The
railways
to
the
east
and
north
-
east
coasts
all
reported
wholesale
damage
done
on
Sunday
night
by
the
advance

of
the
enemy
,
and
now
this
was
continued
on
the
night
of
Monday
in
the
south
,
the
objective
being
to
hinder
troops
from
moving
north
from
Aldershot
.
<EOS>
This
was
,
indeed
,
effectual
,
for
only
by
a
long
_
detour
<EOS>
_
could
the
troops
be
moved
to
the
northern

of
London
,
and
while
many
were
on
Tuesday
entrained
,
others
were
conveyed
to
London
by
the
motor

sent

for

.
 
<EOS>
Everywhere
through
London
and
its
vicinity
,
as
well
as
Manchester
,
Birmingham
,
Sheffield
,
Coventry
,
Leeds
,
and

,
motor
-

and

from

were

by
the

,
for
they
would
,
it

believed
,
replace
cavalry
to

.
 
<EOS>
Wild

were
circulated
regarding

in

north
.
<EOS>
Hull
,
Newcastle
,
Gateshead
,
and
Tynemouth
had
,

,

.
<EOS>
The

in

was
burning
,
and

Elswick

by

.
<EOS>
Details

,

,
very
vague
,
as
the
Germans
were
taking
every

to
prevent

reaching

.
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
III
.
 
<EOS>
NEWS
OF
THE
ENEMY
.
  
<EOS>
Terror
and
excitement
reigned
everywhere
.
<EOS>
The
wildest
rumours
were
hourly
afloat
.
<EOS>
London
was
a
seething
stream
of
breathless
multitudes
of
every
class
.
 
<EOS>
On
Monday
morning
the
newspapers
throughout
the
kingdom
had
devoted
greater
part
of
their
space
to
the
extraordinary
intelligence
from
Norfolk
and
Suffolk
,
and
Essex
,
and
other
places
.
<EOS>
Only
the
slow
,
old
-
fashioned
"
Globe
"
remained
asleep
,
or
pretended
to
know
nothing
of
what
was
in
progress
.
 
<EOS>
That
we
were
actually
invaded
was
plain
,
but
most
of
the
newspapers
happily
preserved
a
calm
,
dignified
tone
,
and
made
no
attempt
at
sensationalism
.
<EOS>
The
situation
was
far
too
serious
.
 
<EOS>
Like
the
public
,
however
,
the
Press
had
been
taken
entirely
by
surprise
.
<EOS>
The
blow
had
been
so
sudden
and
so
staggering
that
half
the
alarming
reports
were
discredited
.
 
<EOS>
In
addition
to
the
details
of
the
enemy
's
operations
,
as
far
as
could
as
yet

ascertained
,
the
"
Morning

"

contained

of
a

at
Chatham
,
which
read

:
     
"
CHATHAM
,
_
Sept.
1
_
(
<EOS>
11.30

)
.
 
<EOS>
"
An

took

on

about

.
<EOS>
The

'
Pole

,
'
1,200

,

,

,

'

,
'

,

,

.
<EOS>
Various

,

<EOS>

'

'

'

.
<EOS>
The
'

'

,
and

.
<EOS>
Tugs

carrying

,

,

,

,
steps

.
<EOS>
In

such

directly

to
one

-
ports
is

,

.
<EOS>
"
     
<EOS>
"
_
Sept
.
2
.
<EOS>
_
 
<EOS>
"
An
extraordinary
_
denouement
_
has
followed
the
collision
in
the
Medway
reported
in
my

last

,
which
renders

.
<EOS>
Everything

to

that

premeditated

of
an

with

'

'
the

men
-

-

.
<EOS>
In

Scripture
,
'
An

hath

,
'

there

.
<EOS>
It

an

to

what

in

an

of

,
however

.
<EOS>
We

'

,
even

known

.
<EOS>
We

,

,

,

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
"
As

the

Kethole
Reach

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

'

.
 
<EOS>
"
Among

'

,
'

<EOS>

<EOS>
Ten

<EOS>

<EOS>
An

"

'

Ver

,
about
fifty
more
started
.
<EOS>
Then
larger
bodies
went
forward
,
but
more
and
more
horses
kept
going
ashore
,
as
though
their
number
was
never
-
ending
.
<EOS>
They
must
have
been
stowed
mighty
close
,
and
many
of
the
ships
must
have
been
specially
fitted
up
for
them
.
 
<EOS>
"
Very
soon
I
saw
cavalry
swarming
up
over
Muckleburgh
,
Warborough
,
and
Telegraph
Hills
,
while
a
good
many
trotted
away
in
the
direction
of
Runton
and
Sheringham
.
<EOS>
Then
,
soon
after
they
had
gone
--
that
is
,
in
about
an
hour
and
a
half
from
their
first

--
the

began
to
move
off
,
and
as
far
as
I
could
see
,
they
marched
inland
by
every

,
some
in
the
direction
of
Kelling
Street
and
Holt
,
others
over
Weybourne

towards
Bodham
,
and
still

woods
over
to
Upper
Sheringham
.
<EOS>
Large

of

marched
along
the
Sheringham
Road
,
and
seemed
to
have
a

of
officers
on

,
while
up

Muckleburgh
Hill
I

frantic

.
 
<EOS>
"
By
this
time
they
had
a
quantity
of
carts
and
wagons
landed
,
and
a
large
number
of
motor
-
cars
.
<EOS>
The
latter
were
soon
started
,
and
,
manned
by
infantry
,
moved
swiftly
in
procession
after
the
troops
.
<EOS>
The
great
idea
of
the
Germans
was
apparently
to
get
the
beach
clear
of
everything
as
soon
as
landed
,
for
all
stores
,
equipment
,
and
other
tackle
were
pushed
inland
as
soon
as
disembarked
.
 
<EOS>
"
The
enemy
kept
on
landing
.
<EOS>
Thousands
of
soldiers
got
ashore
without
any
check
,
and
all
proceeding
orderly
and
without
the
slightest
confusion
,
as
though
the
plans
were
absolutely
perfect
.
<EOS>
Everybody
seemed
to
know
exactly
what
to
do
.
<EOS>
From
where
we
were
we
could
see
the
coastguards
held
prisoners
in
their
station
,
with
German
sentries
mounted
around
;
and
as
the
tide
was
now
setting
strong
to
the
westward
,
Ted
<EOS>
and
I
just
let
our
anchor
off
the
ground
and
allowed

to
drift
.
<EOS>
It
occurred
to
me
that
perhaps
I
might
be
able

the

at
some
other
coastguard
station
if

only

away

in
the
busy
scene
now
in

.
 
<EOS>
"
That
the
Germans
had
actually
landed
in
England
now
apparent
;
yet
we
wondered
what
our
own
fleet
could
be
doing
,
and
pictured
to

the
jolly
good
drubbing
that
our
cruisers
would
give
the
audacious
foreigner
when
they
did
haul
in
sight
.
<EOS>
It
was
for
us
,
at
all
costs
,
to
give
the
alarm
,
so
gradually
we
drifted
off
to
the
nor'-westward
,
in
fear
every
moment
lest
we
should
be
noticed
and
fired
at
.
<EOS>
At
last
we
got
around
Blakeney
Point
successfully
,
and
breathed
more

;
then
hoisting
our
sail
,
we
headed
for
Hunstanton
,
but
seeing
numbers
of
ships
entering
the
Wash
,
and

,
we
put

into

,
where
I

the
chief

of
coastguards
and
told

all

of
that

morning
.
<EOS>
"
 
<EOS>
The
report
added
that
the
officer
of
coastguard
in
question
had
,
three
hours
before
,
noticed
strange

coming
up
the

,
and
had

report

at

,
but

.
<EOS>
An

later
,
however
,
it
had
become
apparent
that
a
still
further
landing
was
being
effected
on
the
south
side
of
the
Wash
,
in
all
probability
at
King
's

.
 
<EOS>
The
fisherman
Scotney
's
statement
had
been
sent
by

from
Wainfleet
on
Sunday

,
but
owing
to
the

of

,

was

reach
the

of
the

in

,

,
until
Monday
.
<EOS>
The
report
received
by
the
Admiralty
had
been
treated
as

until

,
lest
undue

.
 
<EOS>
It
had
then
been
given
to
the
Press
as
revealing
the
truth

had
actually

.
 
<EOS>
The
enemy
had
entered
by
the
back
door
of
England
,
and
the
sensation
it
caused
everywhere
was
little

.
 
<EOS>
Some

very

was

the
Intelligence
Department
of
the
War
Office
,
revealing
the
military

of

who
had

.
 
<EOS>
The
whole
of
the
IVth
German

,
about
38,000

,
had

at
Weybourne
,

,

.
<EOS>
It

,

-

.
<EOS>
The

,

of

-

's

,

,

,

.
<EOS>

No
.

,

.
 
<EOS>
In

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

.
 
<EOS>
Careful

that

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
This

;
indeed
,

.
<EOS>
The

for

to
the
south

down
to

plain
,
while
the
rear

completely
protected
,
so
that
the

could
proceed
until

.

<EOS>
Artillery

,

,

.
<EOS>
This

.
<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
Their

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
In

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

<EOS>
These

,

<EOS>
In

,

,
that

,

.
<EOS>
Cuirassiers

,

--

,

,

's

,

.
 
<EOS>
From

that

six

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Three

,

.
<EOS>
At

,

,

's

,

.
 
<EOS>
That

were

at

,

.
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
V.
 
STATE
OF
SIEGE
DECLARED
.
  
<EOS>
That
our
fleet
had
been
taken
unawares
was
apparent
.
<EOS>
There
were
all
sorts
of
vague

of

upon
the
North
Sea
Fleet
at

,

,

had

by
Germany
.
<EOS>
It
is
,

,
the
land

which
we
have
here
to

.
 
<EOS>
The
authentic

of
a

in

--
somewhere

--
was
now

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

.
<EOS>
This

had
,

,

.
 
<EOS>
It
read
as

<EOS>
"
On
Sunday

,
September
2
,
I
had

to

with

Somers
,

,

.
<EOS>
I

at
the
Golf
Hut
about
8.30
.
<EOS>
We
played
one
round
,
and
were
at
the
last

three
in

when

.
<EOS>
We
could

anything

all

,
and

so

,

before

.
<EOS>
I

making

when
an

from

spoilt

.
<EOS>
I

,
but

--

--
my

was

.
 
<EOS>
"
'
Who
on
earth

fellows
?
'
<EOS>
he

.
<EOS>
As

,

to

.
<EOS>
Galloping

three
men
in

--

,

.
<EOS>
I

,

-

.
 
<EOS>
"

were

,
pulling

with
a

,

.
<EOS>

,

,

,

ass

,
required

to
'
surrender
'
in
quite

pompous

,
but
in
very
good
English
.

;

,
according
to
the
papers
,
hardly
any
troops
had
as
yet
assembled
at
their
places
of
concentration
.
<EOS>
The
whole
of
the
east
of
England
was
helplessly
in
the
invader
's
hands
.
<EOS>
From
Newcastle
had
come
terrible
reports
of
the
bombardment
.
<EOS>
Half
the
city
was
in
flames
,
the
Elswick
works
were
held
by
the
enemy
,
and
whole
streets
in
Newcastle
,
Gateshead
,
Sunderland
,
and
Tynemouth
were
still
burning
fiercely
.
 
<EOS>
The
Tynemouth
fort
had
proved
of
little
or
no
use
against
the
enemy
's
guns
.
<EOS>
The
Germans
had
,
it
appeared
,
used
petrol
bombs
with
appalling
results
,
spreading
fire
,
disaster
,
and
death
everywhere
.
<EOS>
The
inhabitants
,
compelled
to
fly
with
only
the
clothes
they
wore
,
had
scattered
all
over
Northumberland
and
Durham
,
while
the
enemy
had
seized
a
quantity
of
valuable
shipping
that
had
been
in
the
Tyne
,
hoisted
the
German
flag
,
and
converted
the
vessels
to
their
own
uses
.
 
<EOS>
Many
had
already
been
sent
across
to
Wilhelmshaven
,
Emden
,
Bremerhaven
,
and
other
places
to
act
as
transports
,
while
the
Elswick
works
--
which
surely
ought
to
have
been
properly
protected
--
supplied
the
Germans
with
quantities
of
valuable
material
.
 
<EOS>
Panic
and
confusion
were
everywhere
.
<EOS>
All
over
the
country
the
railway
system
was
utterly
disorganised
,
business
everywhere
was
at
a
complete
deadlock
,
for
in
every
town
and
city
all
over
the
kingdom
the
banks
were
closed
.
 
<EOS>
Lombard
Street
,
Lothbury
,
and
other
banking
centres
in
the
City
<EOS>
had
all
day
on
Monday
been
the
scene
of
absolute
panic
.
<EOS>
There
,
as
well
as
at
every
branch
bank
all
over
the
metropolis
,
had
occurred
a
wild
rush
to
withdraw
deposits
by
people
who
foresaw
disaster
.
<EOS>
Many
,
indeed
,
intended
to
fly
with
their
families
away
from
the
country
.
 
<EOS>
The
price
of
the
necessities
of
life
had
risen
further
,
and
in
the
East
End
and
poorer
districts
of
Southwark
the
whole
population
were
already
in
a
state
of
semi
-
starvation
.
<EOS>
But
worst
of
all
,
the
awful
truth
with
which
London
was
now
face
to
face
<EOS>
was
that
the
metropolis
was
absolutely
defenceless
.
 
<EOS>
Every
hour
the
papers
were
appearing
with
fresh
details
of
the
invasion
,
for
reports
were
so
rapidly
coming
in
from
every
hand
that
the
Press
had
difficulty
in
dealing
with
them
.
 
<EOS>
Hull
and
Goole
were
known
to
be
in
the
hands
of
the
invaders
,
and
Grimsby
,
where
the
Mayor
had
been
unable
to
pay
the
indemnity
demanded
,
had
been
sacked
.
<EOS>
But
details
were
not
yet
forthcoming
.
 
<EOS>
Londoners
,
however
,
learnt
late
that
night
more
authentic
news
from
the
invaded
zone
,
of
which
Beccles
was
the
centre
,
and
it
was
to
the
effect
that
those
who
had
landed
at
Lowestoft
were
the
IXth
German
Army
Corps
,
with
General
von
Kronhelm
,
the
Generalissimo
of
the
German
Army
.
<EOS>
This
Army
Corps
,
consisting
of
about
40,000
men
,
was
divided
into
the
17th
Division
,
commanded
by
Lieutenant
-
General
Hocker
,
and
the
18th
by
Lieutenant
-
General
von
Rauch
.
<EOS>
The
cavalry
was
under
the
command
of
Major
-
General
von
Heyden
,
and
the
motor
-
infantry
under
Colonel
Reichardt
.
      
<EOS>
NOTICE
.
     
<EOS>
TO
<EOS>
ALL
GERMAN
SUBJECTS
RESIDENT
IN
ENGLAND
.
     
<EOS>
WILHELM
.
     
<EOS>
To
all
OUR
LOYAL
SUBJECTS
,
GREETING
.
     
<EOS>
We
hereby
COMMAND
and
enjoin
that
all
persons
born
within
the
    
German
Empire
,
or
being
German
subjects
,
whether
liable
to
    
military
service
or
not
,
shall
join
our
arms
at
any
headquarters
    
of
either
of
our
Army
Corps
in
England
within
24
hours
of
the
    
date
of
this
proclamation
.
     
<EOS>
Any
German
subject
failing
to
obey
this
<EOS>
our
Command
will
be
    
treated
as
an
enemy
.
     
<EOS>
By
the
EMPEROR
'S
Command
.
     
<EOS>
Given
at
Beccles
,
Sept.
3rd
,
1910
.
     
<EOS>
=
VON
KRONHELM=
,
    
<EOS>
Commanding
the
Imperial
German
Army
in
England
.
     
<EOS>
[
Illustration
:
FACSIMILE
OF
A
PROCLAMATION
POSTED
BY
UNKNOWN
    
HANDS
<EOS>
ALL
OVER
THE
COUNTRY
.
]
  
<EOS>
According
to
official
information
which
had
reached
the
War
Office
and
been
given
to
the
Press
,
the
17th
Division
was
made
up
of
the
Bremen
and
Hamburg
Infantry
Regiments
,
the
Grand
Duke
of
Mecklenburg
's
Grenadiers
,
the
Grand
Duke
's
Fusiliers
,
the
Luebeck
Regiment
No
.
162
,
the
Schleswig
-
Holstein
Regiment
No
.
163
,
while
the
cavalry
brigade
consisted
of
the
17th
and
18th
Grand
Duke
of
Mecklenburg
's
Dragoons
.
 
<EOS>
The
18th
Division
consisted
of
the
Schleswig
Regiment
No
.
84
and
the
Schleswig
Fusiliers
No
.
86
,
the
Thuringen
Regiment
,
and
the
Duke
of
Holstein
's
Regiment
,
the
two
latter
regiments
being
billeted
in
Lowestoft
,
while
the
cavalry
brigade
forming
the
screen
across
from
Leiston
by
Wilby
to
Castle
Hill
were
Queen
Wilhelmina
's
Hanover
Hussars
and
the
Emperor
of
Austria
's
Schleswig
-
Holstein
Hussars
No
.
16
.
<EOS>
These
,
with
the
smart
motor
infantry
,
held
every
communication
in
the
direction
of
London
.
 
<EOS>
As
far
as
could
be
gathered
,
the
German
commander
had
established
his
headquarters
in
Beccles
,
and
had
not
moved
.
<EOS>
It
now
became
apparent
that
the
telegraph
cables
between
the
East
coast
and
Holland
and
Germany
,
already
described
in
the
first
chapter
,
had
never
been
cut
at
all
.
<EOS>
They
had
simply
been
held
by
the
enemy
's
advance
agents
until
the
landing
had
been
effected
.
<EOS>
And
now
Von
Kronhelm
had
actually
established
direct
communication
between
Beccles
and
Emden
,
and
on
to
Berlin
.
 
<EOS>
Reports
from
the
North
Sea
spoke
of
the
enemy
's
transports
returning
to
the
German
coast
,
escorted
by
cruisers
;
therefore
the
plan
was
undoubtedly
not
to
move
until
a
very
much
larger
force
had
been
landed
.
 
<EOS>
Could
England
regain
her
command
of
the
sea
in
time
to
prevent
the
completion
of
the
blow
?
 
<EOS>
That
night
the
London
streets
presented
a
scene
of
panic
indescribable
.
<EOS>
The
theatres
opened
,
but
closed
their
doors
again
,
as
nobody
would
see
plays
while
in
that
excited
state
.
<EOS>
Every
shop
was
closed
,
and
every
railway
station
was
filled
to
overflowing
with
the
exodus
of
terrified
people
fleeing
to
the
country
westward
,
or
reserves
on
their
way
to
join
the
colours
.
 
<EOS>
The
incredulous
manner
in
which
the
country
first
received
the
news
had
now
been
succeeded
by
wild
terror
and
despair
.
<EOS>
On
that
bright
Sunday
afternoon
they
laughed
at
the
report
as
a
mere
journalistic
sensation
,
but
ere
the
sun
set
<EOS>
the
hard
,
terrible
truth
was
forced
upon
them
,
and
now
,
on
Tuesday
night
,
the
whole
country
,
from
Brighton
to
Carlisle
,
from
Yarmouth
to
Aberystwyth
,
was
utterly
disorganised
and
in
a
state
of
terrified
anxiety
.
 
<EOS>
The
eastern
counties
were
already
beneath
the
iron
heel
of
the
invader
,
whose
objective
was
the
world
's
great
capital
--
London
.
 
<EOS>
Would
they
reach
it
?
<EOS>
That
was
the
serious
question
upon
every
one
's
tongue
that
fevered
,
breathless
night
.
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
VI
.
 
<EOS>
HOW
THE
ENEMY
DEALT
THE
BLOW
.
  
<EOS>
Meanwhile
,
at
the
regimental
depots
feverish
excitement
prevailed
on
Wednesday
,
September
5
,
now
that
every
man
was
ordered
on
active
service
.
<EOS>
All
officers
and
men
who
had
been
on
leave
were
recalled
,
and
medical
inspection
of
all
ranks
at
once
commenced
.
<EOS>
Rations
and
bedding
,
stores
and
equipment
were
drawn
,
but
there
was
a
great
lack
of
uniforms
.
<EOS>
Unlike
the
German
Army
,
where
every
soldier
's
equipment
is
complete
even
to
the
last
button
on
the
proverbial
gaiter
,
and
stowed
away
where
the
owner
knows
where
to
obtain
it
,
our
officers
commanding
depots
commenced
indenting
for
clothing
on
the
Royal
Army
Clothing
Department
and
the
Army
Corps
Clothing
Department
.
 
<EOS>
A
large
percentage
of
men
were
,
of
course
,
found
medically
unfit
to
serve
,
and
were
discharged
to
swell
the
mobs
of
hungry
idlers
.
<EOS>
The
plain
clothes
of
the
reservists
coming
in
were
disposed
of
,
no
man
daring
to
appear
in
the
ranks
unless
in
uniform
.
<EOS>
Von
Kronhelm
's
proclamation
having
forbidden
the
tactics
of
the
Boers
of
putting
mere
armed
citizens
into
the
field
.
 
<EOS>
Horse
-
collecting
parties
went
out
all
over
the
country
,
taking
with
them
head
-
collars
,
head
-
ropes
,
bits
,
reins
,
surcingles
,
numnahs
,
horse
-
blankets
,
and
nose
-
bags
.
<EOS>
These
scoured
every
county
in
search
of
likely
animals
,
every
farm
,
every
livery
stable
,
every
hunting
-
box
,
all
hound
-
kennels
and
private
stables
were
visited
,
and
a
choice
made
.
<EOS>
All
this
,
however
,
took
time
.
<EOS>
Precious
hours
were
thus
being
wasted
while
the
enemy
were
calmly
completing
their
arrangements
for
the
long
-
contemplated
blow
at
the
heart
of
the
British
Empire
.
 
<EOS>
While
the
War
Office
refused
any
information
,
special
editions
of
the
papers
during
Wednesday
printed
sensational
reports
of
the
ruthless
completion
of
the
impenetrable
screen
covering
the
operations
of
the
enemy
on
the
whole
of
the
East
Coast
.
 
<EOS>
News
had
,
by
some
means
,
filtered
through
from
Yarmouth
that
a
similar
landing
to
those
at
Lowestoft
and
Weybourne
had
been
effected
.
<EOS>
Protected
as
such
an
operation
was
by
its
flanks
being
supported
by
the
IVth
and
IXth
Army
Corps
landing
on
either
side
,
the
Xth
Army
Corps
,
under
General
von
Wilburg
,
had
seized
Yarmouth
,
with
its
many
miles
of
wharves
and
docks
,
which
were
now
crowded
by
the
lighters
'
craft
of
flotilla
from
the
Frisian
Islands
.
 
<EOS>
It
was
known
that
the
landing
had
been
effected
simultaneously
with
that
at
Lowestoft
.
<EOS>
The
large
number
of
cranes
at
the
fish
-
docks
were
of
invaluable
use
to
the
enemy
,
for
there
they
landed
guns
,
animals
,
and
stores
,
while
the
provisions
they
found
at
the
various
ship
's
chandlers
,
and
in
such
shops
as
Blagg
's
,
and
the
International
Stores
in
King
Street
,
Peter
Brown
's
,
Doughty
's
,
Lipton
's
,
Penny
's
and
Barnes
's
,
were
at
once
commandeered
.
<EOS>
Great
stores
of
flour
were
seized
in
Clarke
's
and
Press
's
mills
,
while
the
horse
-
provender
mills
in
the
vicinity
supplied
them
with
valuable
forage
.
 
<EOS>
Beyond
these
few
details
,
as
far
as
regarded
the
fate
of
Yarmouth
,
nothing
further
was
at
present
known
.
 
<EOS>
The
British
division
at
Colchester
,
which
comprised
all
the
regular
troops
north
of
the
Thames
in
the
eastern
command
,
was
,
no
doubt
,
in
a
critical
position
,
threatened
so
closely
north
and
south
by
the
enemy
.
<EOS>
None
of
the
regiments
--
the
Norfolks
,
the
Leicestershire
,
and
the
King
's
Own
Scottish
Borderers
of
the
11th
Infantry
Brigade
--
were
up
to
their
strength
.
<EOS>
The
12th
Infantry
Brigade
,
which
also
belonged
to
the
division
,
possessed
only
skeleton
regiments
stationed
at
Hounslow
and
Warley
.
<EOS>
Of
the
4th
Cavalry
Brigade
,
some
were
at
Norwich
,
the
21st
Lancers
were
at
Hounslow
,
while
only
the
16th
Lancers
were
at
Colchester
.
<EOS>
Other
cavalry
regiments
were
as
far
away
as
Canterbury
,
Shorncliffe
,
and
Brighton
,
and
although
there
were
three
batteries
of
artillery
at
Colchester
,
some
were
at
Ipswich
,
others
at
Shorncliffe
,
and
others
at
Woolwich
.
 
<EOS>
Therefore
it
was
quite
evident
to
the
authorities
in
London
that
unless
both
Colchester
and
Norwich
were
instantly
strongly
supported
,
they
would
soon
be
simply
swept
out
of
existence
by
the
enormous
masses
of
German
troops
now
dominating
the
whole
eastern
coast
,
bent
upon
occupying
London
.
 
<EOS>
Helpless
though
they
felt
themselves
to
be
,
the
garrison
at
Colchester
did
all
they
could
.
<EOS>
All
available
cavalry
had
been
pushed
out
past
Ipswich
,
north
to
Wickham
Market
,
Stowmarket
,
and
across
to
Bury
St.
Edmunds
,
only
to
find
on
Wednesday
morning
that
they
were
covering
the
hasty
retreat
of
the
small
body
of
cavalry
who
had
been
stationed
at
Norwich
.
<EOS>
They
,
gallantly
led
by
their
officers
,
had
done
everything
possible
to
reconnoitre
and
attempt
to
pierce
the
enemy
's
huge
cavalry
screen
,
but
in
every
instance
entirely
in
vain
.
<EOS>
They
had
been
outnumbered
by
the
squadrons
of
independent
cavalry
operating
in
front
of
the
Germans
,
and
had
,
alas
!
<EOS>
left
numbers
of
their
gallant
comrades
upon
the
roads
,
killed
and
wounded
.
 
<EOS>
Norwich
had
,
therefore
,
on
Wednesday
morning
fallen
into
the
hands
of
the
German
cavalry
,
utterly
defenceless
.
<EOS>
From
the
Castle
the
German
flag
was
now
flying
,
the
Britannia
Barracks
were
being
used
by
the
enemy
,
food
had
all
been
seized
,
the
streets
were
in
a
state
of
chaos
,
and
a
complete
reign
of
terror
had
been
created
when
a
company
of
British
Infantry
,
having
fired
at
some
Uhlans
,
were
ruthlessly
shot
down
in
the
street
close
by
the
Maid
's
Head
.
 
<EOS>
In
addition
to
this
,
the
Mayor
of
Norwich
was
taken
prisoner
,
lodged
in
the
Castle
,
and
held
as
surety
for
the
well
-
behaviour
of
the
town
.
 
<EOS>
Everywhere
Von
Kronhelm
's
famous
proclamation
was
posted
,
and
as
the
invaders
poured
into
the
city
the
inhabitants
looked
on
in
sullen
silence
,

<EOS>

"
GOOLE
,
_

3
<EOS>
_
.
 
<EOS>
"
Shortly

The

<EOS>
"

<EOS>

<EOS>
"

<EOS>
CITY

.
     
<EOS>

IN

<EOS>

.
 
<EOS>

<EOS>
DESPERATE

<EOS>

<EOS>
"
DANBURY

_

<EOS>
_
.
 
<EOS>
"
To

.
<EOS>
The

<EOS>
"

<EOS>

Maldon

"

<EOS>
"
CHELMSFORD

.
 
<EOS>
"

<EOS>
"
'

'

"
    
CHAPTER
VIII
.
 
<EOS>
DEFENCE
AT
LAST
.
  
<EOS>
Late
on
Wednesday
night
came
tardy
news
of
the
measures
we
were
taking
to
mobilise
.
 
<EOS>
The
Aldershot
Army
Corps
,
so
complete
in
the
"
Army
List
,
"
<EOS>
consisted
,
as
all
the
world
knew
,
of
three
divisions
,
but
of
these
only
two
existed
,
the
other
being
found
to
be
on
paper
.
<EOS>
The
division
in
question
,
located
at
Bordon
,
was
to
be
formed
on
mobilisation
,
and
this
measure
was
now
being
proceeded
with
.
<EOS>
The
train

was

,
owing
to
the

to
the
various
lines
south
of
London
by
the
enemy
's

.
<EOS>
Several
of
these
men
had
been
detected
,
and
,
being
in
plain

,
were
promptly
shot
out
of
hand
.
<EOS>
However
,
their
work
had
,

,

,

,

<EOS>

,

<EOS>
There

.
 
<EOS>
Before

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
What

<EOS>

The

<EOS>
It

.
<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>

,
St.

,
_
en

_

.
<EOS>
Roughly
,

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
The

<EOS>
In

.
 
<EOS>
Following

-

,

,

<EOS>
No

,

,

<EOS>
The

.
 
<EOS>
If

,

<EOS>
But

?
 
<EOS>
This

's

all

the

,
for

now

that

.
 
<EOS>
It

!
 
<EOS>
The

.
 
<EOS>
London
was

of

and

.
<EOS>
In

,

was
now

.
<EOS>
The

system
had
received
a
fatal

,
and
nobody

buy

.
<EOS>
Had

there
would
have

a

,
but
,

,

that

.
<EOS>
Even
Consols
were
now

.
<EOS>
Some

were
known

have

,
and

,

,

.
<EOS>
Only

,

.
 
<EOS>
The

held

was
that

should
unfortunately

to

,
and
Germany
demand

,
there

still

,
however

.
<EOS>
The
experience
of

Franco
-

had
proved
that
,
though

such

the
Bank
,

,
might

,
yet
,

,

.
<EOS>
During

of

,
the

never

.
,

was
5
,
4
,

.
<EOS>
Therefore
what
the

by
sound

had
been
able
to
do
,
there
was
no

why
English

could
not
also

.
      
<EOS>
WE
,
WILHELM
,
     
GIVE
NOTICE
to
the
inhabitants
of
those
provinces
occupied
by
    
the
German
Imperial
Army
,
that--
     
<EOS>
I
MAKE
WAR
upon
the
soldiers
,
and
not
upon
English
citizens
.
    
<EOS>
Consequently
,
it
is
my
wish
to
give
the
latter
and
their
    
property
entire
security
,
and
as
long
as
they
do
not
embark
upon
    
hostile
enterprise
against
the
German
troops
they
have
a
right
    
to
my
protection
.
     
<EOS>
GENERALS
COMMANDING
the
various
corps
in
the
various
districts
    
in
England
are
ordered
to
place
before
the
public
the
stringent
    
measures
which
I
have
ordered
to
be
adopted
against
towns
,
    
villages
,
and
persons
who
act
in
contradiction
to
the
usages
of
    
war
.
<EOS>
They
are
to
regulate
in
the
same

all
the
operations
    
necessary
for
the
well
-
being
of
our
troops
,
to
fix
the
    
difference
between
the
English
and
German
rate
of

,
and
    
to
facilitate
in
every
manner
possible
the
individual
    
transactions
between
our
Army
and
the
inhabitants
of
England
.

<EOS>
WILHELM.=

Given
at
POTSDAM
,
_
September
4th
,
1910
<EOS>

East

<EOS>

French
Three

.

.
<EOS>

<EOS>
With

<EOS>

<EOS>
Firms

<EOS>

<EOS>
Therefore

<EOS>
Despair

,

!
<EOS>
everywhere
,

.
 
<EOS>
Most

.
<EOS>
The

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>
Bands

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

"

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
From

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Day

hour

terror
increased
.
 
<EOS>
Every

arriving
from
the
North
was
crowded
with
Volunteers
and
troops
of
the
line
from
all

in

Northern

.
<EOS>
The
1st
Battalion
West
Riding
Regiment
had
joined
the
Yorkshire
Light
Infantry
,
who

already
stationed
in
Sheffield
,
as

19th
Hussars
,
and

every

of

.
<EOS>
From
Carlisle

the

of
the
Border

,
from

those
of
the
Yorkshire
Regiment
,

of

Durham

,
and

,

the

,

,
and

of

of

.
<EOS>
Contingents
of

East

.
<EOS>
The

,

of

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
From

<EOS>
The

,

's

,

,

,

,

's

,

.
 
<EOS>
These

,

,

,

,
created
the
utmost

.
<EOS>
The
great

of
idlers
cheered
and

,
the

being

when
each

forming
up
was

away

to

for

,

now

from
Woodhouse
on

,

the
whole

of

Rother
,
through
Catcliffe
,

,

,

,

Greasborough
to
the

,

and

through

,
and

spanned

--
a

eight

.
 
<EOS>
The

was

back
another

to
Norton
,
in

,

.
 
<EOS>
The

line
,
then
,
to

occupied

was
about

,
and
into

was
crowded
the

of
troops
of

.
<EOS>
The
post
of
honour
was
at
Catcliffe
,
the
dominating
key
to
the
whole
position
,
which
was
occupied
by

of
the
1st
Battalion
West

and
the

Yorkshire
Light
Infantry
,
while

which
lay
between
Sheffield
and
the

were

the
guns
of
the
7th
Brigade
Royal

Artillery
,
and
of

Field
Artillery
,

2nd
,

30th
,

,

Brigades
,

.
 
<EOS>
All

of
these

which

of

,

from
the

at

,
were
assembled
the
Volunteers
,
all
now
by

on

busily

in

-

and

.
<EOS>
The

of
this

had
merged

into

of
the
Northern
Command
,
which
nominally
had

in
York
,
but

now

to
Sheffield

,
for

--
that

no

,
and

.
<EOS>
General
Sir
George
Woolmer
,
who
so

in

,
had
therefore

to

,
but

to

,

,

,
moved

to
Handsworth
,
which

.
 
<EOS>
In
the
command
were
to
be
found
roughly
twenty
-
three

of
Militia
and
forty
-
eight
of
Volunteers
;
but
,

the

and

of
the
Government
,
the
former

now
found
themselves
,
at
the
moment
when

,
greatly

of

,
and
,

any

,

in

.
<EOS>
As

,

were
even

,
only
about
fifteen
thousand
having
responded
to

arms
.
<EOS>
And
upon
these
heroic
men
,
utterly
insufficient
in
point

numbers
,
Sheffield
had
to
rely
for

.
 
<EOS>
Away
to
the
eastward
of
Sheffield
--
exactly
where
was
yet
unknown
--
sixty
thousand
perfectly
equipped
and
thoroughly
trained
German
horse
,
foot
,
and
artillery
,
were
ready
at
any
moment
to
advance
westward
into
our
manufacturing
districts
!
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
IX
.
 
<EOS>
BRITISH
SUCCESS
AT
ROYSTON
.
  
<EOS>
Arrests
of
alleged
spies
were
reported
from
Manchester
and
other
large
towns
.
<EOS>
Most
of
the
prisoners
were
,
however
,
able
to
prove
themselves
naturalised
British
subjects
;
but
several
were
detained
pending
investigation
and
examination
of
correspondence
found
at
their
homes
.
<EOS>
In
Manchester
,
where
there
are
always
a
number
of
Germans
,
it
is
known
that
many
slipped
away
on
Sunday
night
after
the
publication
of
the
news
of
the
invasion
.
 
<EOS>
In
most
of
the
larger
Midland
towns
notices
had
been
issued
by
the
mayors
deprecating
hostility
towards
residents
of
foreign
origin
,
and
stating
that
all
suspicious
cases
were
already
receiving
the
attention
of
the
police
.
 
<EOS>
In
Stafford
the
boot
factories
were
idle
.
<EOS>
In
the
Potteries
all
work
was
at
a
standstill
.
<EOS>
At
Stoke
-
on
-
Trent
,
at
Hanley
,
at
Burslem
,
Tunstall
,
and
Congleton
all
was
chaos
,
and
thousands
upon
thousands
were
already
wanting
bread
.
<EOS>
The
silk
-
thread
industry
at
Leek
was
ruined
,
so
was
the
silk
industry
at
Macclesfield
;
the
great
breweries
at
Burton
were
idle
,
while
the
hosiery
factories
of
Leicester
and
the
boot
factories
of
Northampton
were
all
shut
.
 
<EOS>
With
the
German
troops
threatening
Sheffield
,
Nottingham
was
in
a
state
of
intense
alarm
.
<EOS>
The
lace
and
hosiery
factories
had
with
one
accord
closed
on
Tuesday
,
and
the
great
Market
Place
was
now
filled
day
and
night
by
thousands
upon
thousands
of
unemployed
mill
-
hands
of
both
sexes
.
<EOS>
On
Friday
,
however
,
came
the
news
of
how
Sheffield
had
built
barricades
against
the
enemy
,
and
there
ensued
a
frantic
attempt
at
defence
on
the
part
of
thousands
of
terrified
and
hungry
men
and
women
.
<EOS>
In
their
frenzy
they
sacked
houses
in
order
to
obtain
material
to
construct
the
barricades
,
which
were
,
however
,
built
just
where
the
fancy
took
the
crowd
.
 
<EOS>
The
white
,
interminable
North
Road
,
that
runs
so
straight
from
London
through
York
and
Berwick
to
Edinburgh
,
was
,
with
its
by
-
roads
in
the
Midlands
,
now
being
patrolled
by
British
cavalry
,
and
<EOS>
here
and
there
telegraphists
around
a
telegraph
post
showed
that
those
many
wires
at
the
roadside
were
being
used
for
military
communication
.
 
<EOS>
At
several
points
along
the
road
between
Wansford
Bridge
and
Retford
the
wires
had
been
cut
and
tangled
by
the
enemy
's
agents
,
but
by
Friday
all
had
been
restored
again
.
<EOS>
In
one
spot
,
between
Weston
and
Sutton
-
on
-
Trent
,
eight
miles
south
of
Newark
,
a
trench
had
actually
been
dug
during
the
night
,
the
tube
containing
the
subterranean
telegraph
lines
discovered
,
and
the
whole
system
to
the
North
disorganised
.
<EOS>
Similar
damage
had
been
done
by
German
spies
to
the
line
between
London
and
Birmingham
,
two
miles
south
of
Shipston
-
on
-
Stour
;
and
again
the
line
between
Loughborough
and
Nottingham
had
been
similarly
destroyed
.
 
<EOS>
The
Post
Office
linesmen
had
,
however
,
quickly
made
good
the
damage
everywhere
in
the
country
not
already
occupied
by
the
enemy
,
and
telegraph
and
telephone
communication
North
and
South
was
now
practically
again
in
its
normal
state
.
 
<EOS>
Through
Lincolnshire
the
enemy
's
advance
patrols
had
spread
South
over
every
road
between
the
Humber
and
the
Wash
,
and
in
the
city
of
Lincoln
itself
a
tremendous
sensation
was
caused
when
on
Wednesday
,
market
-
day
,
several
bodies
of
German
motor
-
cyclists
swept
into
the
Stonebow
and
dismounted
at
the
Saracen
's
Head
,
amid
the
crowd
of
farmers
and
dealers
who
had
assembled
there
,
not
,
alas
!
<EOS>
to
do
business
,
but
to
discuss
the
situation
.
<EOS>
In
a
moment
the
city
was
panic
-
stricken
.
<EOS>
From
mouth
to
mouth
the
dread
truth
spread
,
that
the
Germans
were
upon
them
,
and
people
ran
indoors
and
barricaded
themselves
within
their
houses
.
 
<EOS>
A
body
of
Uhlans
came
galloping
proudly
through
the
Stonebow
a
quarter
of
an
hour
later
,
and
halted
in
High
Street
as
though
awaiting
orders
.
<EOS>
Then
in
rapid

troops
seemed
to
arrive
from
all
quarters
,
many
halting
in
the
Cathedral
Close
and
by
Exchequer
Gate
,
and
others
riding
through
the
streets
in
order
to
terrify

.
 
<EOS>
Von
Kronhelm
's
famous
proclamation
was
posted
by
German
soldiers
upon
the
police
station
,
upon
the
Stonebow
,
and
upon
the
door
of
the
grand
old
Cathedral
itself
,
and
before
noon
a
German
officer
accompanied
by
his
staff
called
upon
the
Mayor
,
and
warned
him
that
Lincoln
was
occupied
by
the
German
troops
,
and
that
any
armed

would
be
punished
by
death
,
as
the
Generalissimo
's

stated
.
<EOS>
An
indemnity
was
demanded
,
and
then
the
powerless
people
saw
upon
the
Cathedral
and
upon
several
of
the
public

the
German
flag
rise
and
float

upon

summer

.
 
<EOS>
Boston
was
full
of
German
infantry
,
and
officers
had
taken
up

quarters
in
the
Peacock
and
other
hotels
in
the
market
-
place
,
while
upon
the
"
Stump
"
the
enemy
's
colours
were
flying
.
 
<EOS>
No
news
came
from
London
.
<EOS>
People
in
Norwich
,
Ipswich
,
Yarmouth
,
and
other
places
heard
vaguely
of
the
invasion
in
the
North
,
and
of
fighting
in
which
the
Germans
were
careful
to
report
that
they
were
always
successful
.
<EOS>
They
saw
the
magnificently
equipped
army
of
the
Kaiser
,
and
,
comparing
it
with
our
mere
apology
for
military
force
,
regarded
the
issue
as
hopeless
from
the
very
first
.
<EOS>
In
every
town
the
German
colours
were
displayed
,
and
all
kinds
of
placards
in
German
and
in
English
made

.

<EOS>
The
"
Daily

,
"

,

:
     
"
ROYSTON
,
_
September
<EOS>
_
9
.
 
<EOS>
"
Victory
at
last
.
<EOS>
A
victory
due
not
only
to
the
bravery
and
exertion
of
our
troops
,
regular
and

,
but
also
to
the
genius
of
Field
-
Marshal
Lord
Byfield
,
our
Commander
-
in
-
Chief
,
ably
seconded
by
the
energy

with

Sir
William
Packington
,
in
command
of
the
IVth
Army
Corps
at

,
carried
out
that

of
the
programme
entrusted
to
him
.
 
<EOS>
"
But
though
in
this
success
we
may
hope
that
we
are
seeing
the
first
glimmerings
of
dawn
--
of
deliverance
from
the
nightmare
of
German
invasion
that
is
now
oppressing
our
dear
old
England
<EOS>
--
we
must
not

led
into
foolishly

.
<EOS>
The
snake
has
been
scotched
,
and
pretty
badly
into
the
bargain
,
but
he
is
far
from

.
<EOS>
The
German
IVth
Army
Corps
,
under
the
famous
General
Von
Kleppen
,
their
magnificent
Garde
Corps
commanded
by
the
Duke

Mannheim
,
and
Froelich
's
fine
Cavalry
Division
,
have
been

in

on

near
Royston
and

Walden
,
and
driven
back

.
<EOS>
But
we
are
too
weak
to
follow
up
our
victory
as
it
should

followed
up
.
 
<EOS>
"
The
menace
of
the
IXth
and
Xth
Corps
on
our
right
flank
ties
us
to
our
selected
position
,
and
the
bulk
of
our
forces
being
composed
of
indifferently
trained
Volunteers
and
Militia
,
is
much
more
formidable
behind
entrenchments
than
when
attempting
to
manoeuvre
in
a
difficult
and
intricate
country
such
as
it
is
about
here
.
<EOS>
But
,
on
the
other
hand
,
we
have
given
pause
to
the
invaders
,
and
have
certainly
gained
a
few
days
'
time
,
which
will
be
invaluable
to
us
.
 
<EOS>
"
We
shall
be
able
to
get
on
with
the
line
of
fortifications
that
are
being
constructed
to
bar
the
approaches
to
London
,
and
behind

will
be
necessary
for
us
to
make

stand
.
<EOS>
I
do
not
conceive
that
it

possible
for
such

of
amateur

as
ours
are
in
the
main
to
defeat

such

-
trained
forces
as
the
Germans
have
succeeded
in

into

.
<EOS>
But
when
our
Navy
has
regained
command
of
the
sea
,
we
hope
that
we
may
,
before
very

,
place
our
unwelcome
visitors
'
between
the
devil
and
the
deep
sea'--the
part
of
the

being
played
by
our
brave
troops
finally

behind
the

<EOS>
I
heard
a
sharp

and

behind

,
and
,

,
saw
the

and

.
<EOS>
They

,

.
<EOS>
As

,

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
"
It
was
the
2nd
Dorsetshire
.
<EOS>
I

to
see
which

turned
.
<EOS>
As

more
than
expected
,

turned
in

.
<EOS>
My

,

's

,

.
<EOS>
The

;

,

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
With

;

.
<EOS>
I

,

,

,

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
"
During

night

the

came

the

,
while

,

Kronhelm
's

,

.
<EOS>
After

gathering

through
the
mud
,
which
after

's

still

,

that

.
<EOS>
I

just

.
 
<EOS>
"
The

as
the
last

the
ammunition
and

.
<EOS>
The

,

's

.
<EOS>
There

,

--

,

.
<EOS>
Many

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
I

,

.
<EOS>
It

before

.
<EOS>
In

,

,

,

's

,

<EOS>
Luckily

<EOS>
The

,

just

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

key

at

,

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
"
I

.
'
<EOS>
Bless

,
<EOS>
sir
,
I

're

,
'

.
'

,

.
'
<EOS>
The

.
 
<EOS>
"
The

.
<EOS>
But

,

,

,

<EOS>
As

,

,

.
<EOS>
It

,

I

.
<EOS>
The

,

.
<EOS>
I

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
"
Eventually
this

ordered

him
<EOS>
,

;
but

,
'
I

.
<EOS>
In

.
<EOS>
I

,

,

.
'
<EOS>
I

,

very

,

.
<EOS>
In

.
<EOS>
So

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
My

,

.
<EOS>
My

,

.
<EOS>
Thus
,

,

's

,

.
'
<EOS>
What
's

?
'
<EOS>
I

.
'
<EOS>
Oh
,

,
'

.
'
<EOS>
There
's
the

.
'
 
<EOS>
"
So

.
<EOS>
The

had

after

,

.
'
<EOS>
They

,
'

.
 
<EOS>
"
A

,

'
<EOS>
That

16th
,
'

,
'
going

.
'
 
<EOS>
"
So

,

.
<EOS>
We

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
But

,

,

.
<EOS>
As

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
I

.
<EOS>
We

,

,

.
"
      
<EOS>
NOTICE
.
     
<EOS>
CONCERNING
WOUNDED
BRITISH
SOLDIERS
.
     
<EOS>
In
compliance
with
an
order
of
the
Commander
-
in
-
Chief
    
of
the
German
Imperial
Army
,
the
Governor
-
General
    
of
East
Anglia
decrees
as
follows:--
     
<EOS>
(
1
)
<EOS>
Every
inhabitant
of
the
counties
of
Norfolk
,
    
Suffolk
,
Essex
,
Cambridge
,
Lincolnshire
,
Yorkshire
,
    
Nottingham
,
Derby
,
Leicester
,
Northampton
,
Rutland
,
    
Huntingdon
,
and
Hertford
,
who
gives
asylum
to
or
    
lodges
one
or
more
ill
or
wounded
British
soldier
,
is
    
obliged
to
make
a
declaration
to
the
mayor
of
the
town
    
or
to
the
local
police
within
24
hours
,
stating
name
,
    
grade
,
place
of
birth
,
and
nature
of
illness
or
injury
.
     
<EOS>
Every
change
of
domicile
of
the
wounded
is
also
to
    
be
notified
within
24
hours
.
     
<EOS>
In
absence
of
masters
,
servants
are
ordered
to
make
    
the
necessary
declarations
.
     
<EOS>
The
same
order
applies
to
the
directors
of
hospitals
,
    
surgeries
,
or
ambulance
stations
,
who
receive
the
    
British
wounded
within
our
jurisdiction
.
     
<EOS>
(
2
)
<EOS>
All
mayors
are
ordered
to
prepare
lists
of
the
    
British
wounded
,
showing
the
number
,
with
their
    
names
,
grade
,
and
place
of
birth
in
each
district
.
     
<EOS>
(
3
)
<EOS>
The
mayor
,
or
the
superintendent
of
police
,
must
    
send
on
the
1st
and
15th
of
each
month
a
copy
of
    
his
lists
to
the
headquarters
of
the
Commander
-
in
-
Chief
.
    
<EOS>
The
first
list
must
be
sent
on
the
15th
September
.
     
<EOS>
(
4
)
<EOS>
Any
person
failing
to
comply
with
this
order
    
will
,
in
addition
to
being
placed
under
arrest
for
    
harbouring
British
troops
,
be
fined
a
sum
not
exceeding
    
L20
.
     
<EOS>
(
5
)
<EOS>
This
decree
is
to
be
published
in
all
towns
and
    
villages
in
the
Province
of
East
Anglia
.
     
<EOS>

,

-

,

<EOS>
CHAPTER
XI
.
 
<EOS>
FIERCE
FIGHTING
AT
CHELMSFORD
.
  
<EOS>
A
despatch
from
Mr.
Edgar
Hamilton
to
the
"
Daily
News
,
"
as
follows
,
was
published
on
Saturday
,
15th
September
:
 
<EOS>
"
At
Little
Waltham
I
found
myself
close
to
the
scene
of
action
.
<EOS>
About
a
mile
ahead
of
me
the
hamlet
of
Howe
Street
was
in
flames
and
burning
furiously
.
<EOS>
I
could
see
the
shells
bursting
in
and
all
over
it
in
perfect
coveys
.
<EOS>
I
could
not
make
out
where
they
were
coming
from
,
but
an
officer
I
met
said
he
thought
the
enemy
must
have
several
batteries
in
action
on
the
high
ground
about
Littley
Green
,
a
mile
and
a
half
to
the
north
on
the
opposite
side
of
the
river
.
<EOS>
I
crossed
over
myself
,
and
got
up
on
the
knoll
where
the
Leicestershires
and
Dorsets
had
been
stationed
,
together
with
a
number
of
the
4.7-inch
guns
brought
from
Colchester
.
 
<EOS>
"
This
piece
of
elevated
ground
is
about
two
miles
long
,
running
almost
north
and
south
,
and
at
the
top
of
it
I
got
an
extensive
view
to
the
eastward
right
away
to
beyond
Witham
,
as
the
ground
fell
all
the
way
.
<EOS>
The
country
was
well
wooded
,
and
a
perfect
maze
of
trees
and
hedgerows
.
<EOS>
If
there
were
any
Germans
down
there
in
this
plain
they
were
lying
very
low
indeed
,
for
my
glasses
did
not
discover
the
least
indication
of
their
presence
.
<EOS>
Due
east
my
view
was
bounded
by
the
high
wooded
ground
about
Wickham
Bishops
and
Tiptree
Heath
,
which
lay
a
long
blue
hummock
on
the
horizon
,
while
to
the
south
-
east
Danbury
Hill
,
with
our
big
war
-
balloon
floating
overhead
,
was

<EOS>
DECREE
     
CONCERNING
THE
POWER
OF
COUNCILS
OF
WAR
.
     
<EOS>
WE
,
GOVERNOR
-
GENERAL
OF
EAST
ANGLIA
,
in
virtue
of
the
powers
    
conferred
upon
us
by
His
Imperial
Majesty
the
German
Emperor
,
    
Commander
-
in
-
Chief
of
the
German
Armies
,
order
,
for
the
    
maintenance
of
the
internal
and
external
security
of
the
    
counties
of
the
Government
-
General:--
     
ARTICLE
I.--Any
individual
guilty
of
incendiarism
or
of
wilful
    
inundation
,
of
attack
,
or
of
resistance
with
violence
against
    
the
Government
-
General
or
the
agents
of
the
civil
or
military
    
authorities
,
of
sedition
,
of
pillage
,
of
theft
with
violence
,
of
    
assisting
prisoners
to
escape
,
or
of
exciting
soldiers
to
    
treasonable
acts
,
shall
be
PUNISHED
BY
DEATH
.
     
<EOS>
In
the
case
of
any
extenuating
circumstances
,
the
culprit
may
be
    
sent
to
penal
servitude
with
hard
labour
for
twenty
years
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
II.--Any
person
provoking
or
inciting
an
individual
to
    
commit
the
crimes
mentioned
in
Article
I.
will
be
sent
to
penal
    
servitude
with
hard
labour
for
ten
years
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
III.--Any
person
propagating
false
reports
relative
to
    
the
operations
of
war
or
political
events
will
be
imprisoned
for
    
one
year
,
and
fined
up
to
L100
.
     
<EOS>
In
any
case
where
the
affirmation
or
propagation
may
cause
    
prejudice
against
the
German
Army
,
or
against
any
authorities
or
    
functionaries
established
by
it
,
the
culprit
will
be
sent
to
    
hard
labour
for
ten
years
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
IV.--Any
person
usurping
a
public
office
,
or
who
commits
    
any
act
or
issues
any
order
in
the
name
of
a
public
functionary
,
    
will
be
imprisoned
for
five
years
,
and
fined
L150
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
V.--Any
person
who
voluntarily
destroys
or
abstracts
any
    
documents
,
registers
,
archives
,
or
public
documents
deposited
in
    
public
offices
,
or
passing
through
their
hands
in
virtue
of
    
their
functions
as
government
or
civic
officials
,
will
be
    
imprisoned
for
two
years
,
and
fined
L150
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
VI.--Any
person
obliterating
,
damaging
,
or
tearing
down
    
official
notices
,
orders
,
or
proclamations
of
any
sort
issued
by
    
the
German
authorities
will
be
imprisoned
for
six
months
,
and
    
fined
L80
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
VII.--Any
resistance
or
disobedience
of
any
order
given
    
in
the
interests
of
public
security
by
military
commanders
and
    
other
authorities
,
or
any
provocation
or
incitement
to
commit
    
such
disobedience
,
will
be
punished
by
one
year
's
imprisonment
,
    
or
a
fine
of
not
less
than
L150
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
VIII.--All
offences
enumerated
in
Articles
I.--VII
.
<EOS>
are
    
within
the
jurisdiction
of
the
Councils
of
War
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
IX.--It
is
within
the
competence
of
Councils
of
War
to
    
adjudicate
upon
all
other
crimes
and
offences
against
the
    
internal
and
external
security
of
the
English
provinces
occupied
    
by
the
German
Army
,
and
also
upon
all
crimes
against
the
    
military
or
civil
authorities
,
or
their
agents
,
as
well
as
    
murder
,
the
fabrication
of
false
money
,
of
blackmail
,
and
all
    
other
serious
offences
.
     
<EOS>
Article
X.--Independent
of
the
above
<EOS>
,
the
military
jurisdiction
    
already
proclaimed
will
remain
in
force
regarding
all
actions
    
tending
to
imperil
the
security
of
the
German
troops
,
to
damage
    
their
interests
,
or
to
render
assistance
to
the
Army
of
the
    
British
Government
.
     
<EOS>
Consequently
,
there
will
be
PUNISHED
BY
DEATH
,
and
we
expressly
    
repeat
this
,
all
persons
who
are
not
British
soldiers
<EOS>
and--
     
<EOS>
(
a
)
<EOS>
Who
serve
the
British
Army
or
the
Government
as
spies
,
or
    
receive
British
spies
,
or
give
them
assistance
or
asylum
.
     
<EOS>
(
b
)
<EOS>
Who
serve
as
guides
to
British
troops
,
or
mislead
the
German
    
troops
when
charged
to
act
as
guides
.
     
<EOS>
(
c
)
<EOS>
Who
shoot
,
injure
,
or
assault
any
German
soldier
or
officer
.
     
<EOS>
(
d
)
<EOS>
Who
destroy
bridges
or
canals
,
interrupt
railways
or
    
telegraph
lines
,
render
roads
impassable
,
burn
munitions
of
war
,
    
provisions
,
or
quarters
of
the
troops
.
     
<EOS>
(
e
)
<EOS>
Who
take
arms
against
the
German
troops
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
XI.--The
organisation
of
Councils
of
War
mentioned
in
    
Articles
VIII
.
<EOS>
and
IX
.
<EOS>
of
the
Law
of
May
2
,
1870
,
and
their
    
procedure
are
regulated
by
special
laws
which
are
the
same
as
    
the
summary
jurisdiction
of
military
tribunals
.
<EOS>
In
the
case
of
    
Article
X.
there
remains
in
force
the
Law
of
July
21
,
1867
,
    
concerning
the
military
jurisdiction
applicable
to
foreigners
.
     
<EOS>
ARTICLE
XII.--The
<EOS>
present
order
is
proclaimed
and
put
into
    
execution
on
the
morrow
of
the
day
upon
which
it
is
affixed
in
    
the
public
places
of
each
town
and
village
,
<EOS>
The
Governor
-
General
    
of
East
Anglia
,
     
=
COUNT
VON
SCHONBURG
-
WALDENBERG
,
Lieutenant
-
General.=
     
NORWICH
,
_
September
7th
_
,
1910
.
  
<EOS>
"
I
had
betaken
myself
at
once
to
the
round
tower
of
the
church
,
next
the
Stone
Bridge
,
from
which
I
had
an
excellent
view
<EOS>
both
east
and
north
.
<EOS>
The
first
thing
that
attracted
my
eye
was
the
myriad
flashings
of
rifle
fire
in
the
dimness
of
the
breaking
day
.
<EOS>
They
reached
in
a
continuous
line
of
coruscations
from
Boreham
Hall
,
opposite
my
right
hand
,
to
the
knoll
by
Little
Waltham
,
a
distance
of
three
or
four
miles
,
I
should
say
.
<EOS>
The
enemy
were
driving
in
all
our
outlying
and
advanced
troops
by
sheer
weight
of
numbers
.
<EOS>
Presently
the
heavy
batteries
at
Danbury
began
pitching
shell
over
in
the
direction
of
the
firing
,
but
as
the
German
line
still
advanced
,
it
had
not
apparently
any
very
great
effect
.
<EOS>
The
next
thing
that
happened
was
a
determined
attack
on
the
village
of
Howe
Street
made
from
the
direction
of
Hyde
Hall
.
<EOS>
This
is
about
two
miles
north
of
Little
Waltham
.
<EOS>
In
spite
of
our
incessant
fire
,
the
Germans
had
contrived
to
mass
a
tremendous
number
of
guns
and
howitzers
on
and
behind
the
knoll
they
captured
last
night
,
and
there
was
any
quantity
more
on
the
ridge
above
Hyde
Hall
.
<EOS>
All
these
terrible
weapons
concentrated
their
fire
for
a
few
moments
on
the
blackened
ruins
of
Howe
Street
.
<EOS>
Not
a
mouse
could
have
lived
there
.
<EOS>
The
little
place
was
simply
pulverised
.
 
<EOS>
"
Our
guns
at
Pleshy
Mount
and
Rolphy
Green
,
aided
by
a
number
of
field
batteries
,
in
vain
endeavoured
to
make
head
against
them
.
<EOS>
They
were
outnumbered
by
six
to
one
.
<EOS>
Under
cover
of
this
tornado
of
iron
and
fire
,
the
enemy
pushed
several
battalions
over
the
river
,
making
use
of
the
ruins
of
the
many
bridges
about
there
which
had
been
hastily
destroyed
,
and
which
they
repaired
with
planks
and
other
materials
they
brought
along
with
them
.
<EOS>
They
lost
a
large
number
of
men
in
the
process
,
but
they
persevered
,
and
by
ten
o'clock
were
in
complete
possession
of
Howe
Street
,
Langley
's
Park
,
and
Great
Waltham
,
and
moving
in
fighting
formation
against
Pleshy
Mount
and
Rolphy
Green
,
their
guns
covering
their
advance
with
a
perfectly
awful
discharge
of
shrapnel
.
<EOS>
Our
cannon
on
the
ridge
at
Partridge
Green
took
the
attackers
in
flank
,
and
for
a
time
checked
their
advance
,
but
,
drawing
upon
themselves
the
attention
of
the
German
artillery
,
on
the
south
end
of
the
knoll
,
were
all
but
silenced
.
 
<EOS>
"
As
soon
as
this
was
effected
another
strong
column
of
Germans
followed
in
the
footsteps
of
the
first
,
and
deploying
to
the
left
,
secured
the
bridge
at
Little
Waltham
,
and
advanced
against
the
gun
positions
on
Partridge
Green
.
<EOS>
This
move
turned
all
our
river
bank
entrenchments
right
down
to
Chelmsford
.
<EOS>
Their
defenders
were
now
treated
to
the
enfilade
fire
of
a
number
of
Hanoverian
batteries
that
galloped
down
to
Little
Waltham
.
<EOS>
They
stuck
to
their
trenches
gallantly
,
but
presently
when
the
enemy
obtained
a
footing
on
Partridge
Green
they
were
taken
in
reverse
,
and
compelled
to
fall
back
,
suffering
terrible
losses
as
they
did
so
.
<EOS>
The
whole
of
the
infantry
of
the
Xth
Corps
,
supported
--
as
we
understand
--
by
a
division
which
had
joined
them
from
Maldon
,
now
moved
down
on
Chelmsford
.
<EOS>
In
fact
,
there
was
a
general
advance
of
the
three
combined
armies
stretching
from
Partridge
Green
on
the
west
to
the
railway
line
on
the
east
.
<EOS>
The
defenders
of
the
trenches
facing
east
were
hastily
withdrawn
,
and
thrown
back
on
Writtle
.
<EOS>
The
Germans
followed
closely
with
both
infantry
and
guns
,
though
they
were
for
a
time
checked
near
Scot
's
Green
by
a
dashing
charge
of
our
cavalry
brigade
,
consisting
of
the
16th
Lancers
and
the
7th
,
14th
,
and
20th
Hussars
,
and
the
Essex
and
Middlesex
Yeomanry
.
<EOS>
We
saw
nothing
of
their
cavalry
,
for
a
reason
that
will
be
apparent
later
.
<EOS>
By
one
o'clock
fierce
fighting
was
going
on
all
round
the
town
,
the
German
hordes
enveloping
it
on
all
sides
but
one
.
<EOS>
We
had
lost
a
great
number
of
our
guns
,
or
at
any
rate
had
been
cut
off
from
them
by
the
German
successes
around
Pleshy
Mount
,
and
in
all
their
assaults
on
the
town
they
had
been
careful
to
keep
out
of
effective
range
of
the
heavy
batteries
on
Danbury
Hill
.
<EOS>
These
,
by
the
way
,
had
their
own
work
cut
out
for
them
,
as
the
Saxon
artillery
were
heavily
bombarding
the
hill
with
their
howitzers
.
<EOS>
The
British
forces
were
in
a
critical
situation
.
<EOS>
Reinforcements
--
such
as
could
be
spared
--
were
hurried
up
from
the
Vth
Army
Corps
,
but
they
were
not
very
many
in
numbers
,
as
it
was
necessary
to
provide
against
an
attack
by
the
Saxon
Corps
.
<EOS>
By
three
o'clock
the
greater
part
of
the
town
was
in
the
hands
of
the
Germans
,
despite
the
gallant
way
in
which
our
men
fought
them
from
street
to
street
,
and
house
to
house
.
<EOS>
A
dozen
fires
were
spreading
in
every
direction
,
and
fierce
fighting
was
going
on
at
Writtle
.
<EOS>
The
overpowering
numbers
of
the
Germans
,
combined
with
their
better
organisation
,
and
the
number
of
properly
trained
officers
at
their
disposal
,
bore
the
British
mixed
Regular
and
Irregular
forces
back
,
and
back
again
.
 
<EOS>
"
Fearful
of
being
cut
off
from
his
line
of
retreat
,
General
Blennerhasset
,
on
hearing
from
Writtle
soon
after
three
that
the
Hanoverians
were
pressing
his
left
very
hard
,
and
endeavouring
to
work
round
it
,
reluctantly
gave
orders
for
the
troops
in
Chelmsford
to
fall
back
on
Widford
and
Moulsham
.
<EOS>
There
was
a
lull
in
the
fighting
for
about
half
an
hour
,
though
firing
was
going
on
both
at
Writtle
and
Danbury
.
<EOS>
Soon
after
four
a
terrible
rumour
spread
consternation
on
every
side
.
<EOS>
According
to
this
,
an
enormous
force
of
cavalry
and
motor
infantry
was
about
to
attack
us
in
the
rear
.
<EOS>
What
had
actually
happened
was
not
quite
so
bad
as
this
,
but
quite
bad
enough
.
<EOS>
It
seems
,
according
to
our
latest
information
,
that
almost
the
whole
of
the
cavalry
belonging
to
the
three
German
Army
Corps
with
whom
we
were
engaged
--
something
like
a
dozen
regiments
,
with
a
proportion
of
horse
artillery
and
all
available
motorists
,
having
with
them
several
of
the
new
armoured
motors
carrying
light
,
quick
-
firing
and
machine
guns
--
had
been
massed
during
the
last
thirty
-
six
hours
behind
the
Saxon
lines
extending
from
Maldon
to
the
River
Crouch
.
<EOS>
During
the
day
they
had
worked
round
to
the
southward
,
and
at
the
time
the
rumour
reached
us
were
actually
attacking
Billericay
,
which
was
held
by
a
portion
of
the
reserves
of
our
Vth
Corps
.
<EOS>
By
the
time
this
news
was
confirmed
the
Germans
were
assaulting
Great
Baddow
,
and
moving
on
Danbury
from
east
,
north
,
and
west
,
at
the
same
time
resuming
the
offensive
all
along
the
line
.
<EOS>
The
troops
at
Danbury
must
be
withdrawn
or
they
would
be
isolated
.
<EOS>
This
difficult
manoeuvre
was
executed
by
way
of
West
Hanningfield
.
<EOS>
The
rest
of
the
Vth
Corps
conformed
to
the
movement
,
the
Guards
Brigade
at
East
Hanningfield
forming
the
rearguard
,
and
fighting
fiercely
all
night
through
with
the
Saxon
troops
,
who
moved
out
on
the
left
flank
of
our
retreat
.
<EOS>
The
wreck
of
the
first
Corps
and
the
Colchester
garrison
was
now
also
in
full
retirement
.
<EOS>
Ten
miles
lay
between
it
and
the
lines
at
Brentwood
,
and
had
the
Germans
been
able
to
employ
cavalry
in
pursuit
,
this
retreat
would
have
been
even
more
like
a
rout
than
it
was
.
<EOS>
Luckily
for
us
the
Billericay
troops
mauled
the
German
cavalry
pretty
severely
,
and
they
were
beset
in
the
close
country
in
that
neighbourhood
by
Volunteers
,
motorists
,
and
every
one
that
the
officer
commanding
at
Brentwood
could
get
together
in
this
emergency
.
 
<EOS>
"
Some
of
them
actually
got
upon
our
line
of
retreat
,
but
were
driven
off
by
our
advance
guard
;
others
came
across
the
head
of
the
retiring
Vth
Corps
,
but
the
terrain
was
all
against
cavalry
,
and
after
nightfall
most
of
them
had
lost
their
way
in
the
maze
of
lanes
and
hedgerows
that
covered
the
countryside
.
<EOS>
Had
it
not
been
for
this
we
should
probably
have
been
absolutely
smashed
.
<EOS>
As
it
was
,
rather
more
than
half
our
original
numbers
of
men
and
guns
crawled
into
Brentwood
in
the
early
morning
,
worn
out
and
dead
-
beat
.
"
 
<EOS>
Reports
from
Sheffield
also
showed
the
position
to
be
critical
.
    
<EOS>
BOOK
II
.
 
<EOS>
THE
SIEGE
OF
LONDON
.
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
I.
 
<EOS>
THE
LINES
OF
LONDON
.
  
<EOS>
The
German
successes
were
continued
in
the
North
and
Midlands
,
and
notwithstanding
the
gallant
defence
of
Sir
George
Woolmer
before
Manchester
and
Sir
Henry
Hibbard
before
Birmingham
,
both
cities
were
captured
and
occupied
by
the
enemy
after
terrible
losses
.
<EOS>
London
,
however
,
was
the
chief
objective
of
Von
Kronhelm
,
and
towards
the
Metropolis
he
now
turned
his
attention
.
 
<EOS>
After
the
defeat
of
the
British
at
Chelmsford
on
that
fateful
Wednesday
,
Lord
Byfield
decided
to
evacuate
his
position
at
Royston
and
fall
back
on
the
northern
section
of
the
London
defence
line
,
which
had
been
under
construction
for
the
last
ten
days
.
<EOS>
These
hasty
entrenchments
,
which
would
have
been
impossible
to
construct
but
for
the
ready
assistance
of
thousands
of
all
classes
of
the
citizens
of
London
and
the
suburbs
,
extended
from
Tilbury
on
the
east
to
Bushey
on
the
west
,
passing
by
the
Laindon
Hills
,
Brentwood
,
Kelvedon
,
North
Weald
,
Epping
,
Waltham
Abbey
,
Cheshunt
,
Enfield
Chase
,
Chipping
Barnet
,
and
Elstree
.
<EOS>
They
were
more
or
less
continuous
,
consisting
for
the
most
part
of
trenches
for
infantry
,
generally
following
the
lines
of
existing
hedgerows
or
banks
,
which
often
required
but
little
improvement
to
transform
them
into
well
-
protected
and
formidable
cover
for
the
defending
troops
.
<EOS>
Where
it
was
necessary
to
cross
open
ground
they
were
dug
deep
and
winding
,
after
the
fashion
adopted
by
the
Boers
in
the
South
African
War
,
so
that
it
would
be
difficult
,
if
not
impossible
,
to
enfilade
them
.
 
<EOS>
Special
bomb
-
proof
covers
for
the
local
reserves
were
also
constructed
at
various
points
,
and
the
ground
in
front
ruthlessly
cleared
of
houses
,
barns
,
trees
,
hedges
,
and
everything
that
might
afford
shelter
to
an
advancing
enemy
.
<EOS>
Every
possible
military
obstacle
was
placed
in
front
of
the
lines
that
time
permitted
,
abattis
,
military
pits
,
wire
entanglements
,
and
small
ground
mines
.
<EOS>
At
the
more
important
points
along
the
fifty
miles
of
entrenchments
,
field
works
and
redoubts
for
infantry
were
built
,
most
of
them
being
armed
with
4.7
or
even
6
and
7.5
in
.
<EOS>
guns
,
which
had
been
brought
from
Woolwich
,
Chatham
,
Portsmouth
,
and
Devonport
,
and
mounted
on
whatever
carriages
could
be
adapted
or
improvised
for
the
occasion
.
 
<EOS>
The
preparation
of
the
London
lines
was
a
stupendous
undertaking
,
but
the
growing
scarceness
and
dearness
of
provisions
assisted
in
a
degree
,
as
no
free
rations
were
issued
to
any
able
-
bodied
man
unless
he
went
out
to
work

<EOS>

<EOS>
Nothing

<EOS>
CHAPTER

.
 
<EOS>
REPULSE
OF
THE
GERMANS
.
  
<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
Von

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
Von

<EOS>
By

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
Things

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>
But

<EOS>

<EOS>
But

<EOS>
He

,

<EOS>

,
which
with
its
commander
,
had
,
it
was
believed
,
been
made
prisoners
.
<EOS>
They
had
been
caught
between
the
39th
German
Infantry
Brigade
and
several
regiments
of
cavalry
,
that
it
was
said
had
arrived
from
the
northward
soon

were
beaten
at
Hatfield
Heath
.
<EOS>
Probably
these
were
the
advanced

of
General
Froelich
's
Cavalry
Brigade
.
"
    
CHAPTER
III
.
 
<EOS>
BATTLE
OF
EPPING
.
  
<EOS>
The
following
is
extracted
from
the
"
Times
"
of
15th
September
:
     
"
EPPING
,
_
14th
<EOS>
September
_
,
_
Evening
_
.
 
<EOS>
"
I
have
spent
a
busy
day
,
but
have
no
very
important
news
to
record
.
<EOS>
After
the
repulse
of
the
German
troops
attacking
Lord
Byfield
's
retreating
army
and
the
arrival
of
our
sorely
harassed
troops
behind
the
Epping
entrenchments
,
we
saw
no
more
of
the
enemy
that
evening
.
<EOS>
All
through
the
night
,
however
,
there
was
the
sound
of
occasional
heavy
gun
firing
from
the
eastward
.
<EOS>
I
have
taken
up
my
quarters
at
the
Bell
,
an
inn
at
the
south
end
of
the
village
,
from
the
back
of
which
I
can
get
a
good
view
to
the
north
-
west
for
from
two
to
four
miles
.
<EOS>
Beyond
that
distance
the
high
ridge
known
as
Epping
Upland
limits
the
prospect
.
<EOS>
The
whole
terrain
is
cut
up
into
fields
of
various
sizes
and
dotted
all
over
with
trees
.
<EOS>
Close
by
is
a
lofty
red
brick
water
-
tower
,
which
has
been
utilised
by
Sir
Stapleton
Forsyth
as
a
signal
station
.
<EOS>
Away
about
a
mile
to
my
left
front
as
I
look
from
the
back
of
the
Bell
a
big
block
of
buildings
stands
prominently
out
on
a
grassy
spur
of
high
ground
.
<EOS>
This
is
Copped
Hall
and
Little
Copped
Hall
.
 
<EOS>
"
Both
mansions
have
been
transformed
into
fortresses
,
which
,
while
offering
little
or
no
resistance
to
artillery
fire
,
will
yet
form
a
tough
nut
for
the
Germans
to
crack
,
should
they
succeed
in
getting
through
our
entrenchments
at
that
point
.
<EOS>
Beyond
,
I
can
just
see
a
corner
of
a
big
earthwork
that
has
been
built
to
strengthen
the
defence
line
,
and
which
has
been
christened
Fort
Obelisk
from
a
farm
of
that
name
,
near
which
it
is
situated
.
<EOS>
There
is
another
smaller
redoubt
on
the
slope
just
below
this
hostelry
,
and
I
can
see
the
gunners
busy
about
the
three
big
khaki
-
painted
guns
which
are
mounted
in
it
.
<EOS>
There
are
a
6-inch
and
two
4.7-inch
guns
I
believe
.
<EOS>
This
morning
our
cavalry
,
consisting
of
a
regiment
of
yeomanry
and
some
mounted
infantry
,
who
had
formed
a
portion
of
Lord
Byfield
's
force
,
went
out
to
reconnoitre
towards
the
north
and
east
.
<EOS>
They
were
not
away
long
,
as
they
were
driven
back
in
every
direction
in
which
they
attempted
to
advance
,
by
superior
forces
of
the
enemy
's
cavalry
,
who
seemed
to
swarm
everywhere
.
 
<EOS>
"
Later
on
,
I
believe
,
some
of
the
German
reiters
became
so
venturesome
that
several
squadrons
exposed
themselves
to
the
fire
of
the
big
guns
in
the
fort
at
Skip
's
Corner
,
and
suffered
pretty
severely
for
their
temerity
.
<EOS>
The
firing
continued
throughout
the
morning
away
to
eastward
.
<EOS>
At
noon
I
thought
I
would
run
down
and
see
if
I
could
find
anything
out
about
it
.
<EOS>
I
therefore
mounted
my
car
and
ran
off
in
that
direction
.
<EOS>
I
found
that
there
was
a
regular
duel
going
on
between
our
guns
at
Kelvedon
Hatch
and
some
heavy
siege
guns
or
howitzers
that
the
enemy
had
got
in
the
neighbourhood
of
the
high
ground
about
Norton
Heath
,
only
about
3,000
yards
distant
from
our
entrenchments
.
<EOS>
They
did
not
appear
to
have
done
us
much
damage
,
but
neither
,
in
all
probability
,
did
we
hurt
them
very
much
,
since
our
gunners
were
unable
to
exactly
locate
the
hostile
guns
.
 
<EOS>
"
When
I
got
back
to
Epping
,
about
three
o'clock
,
I
found
the
wide
single
street
full
of
troops
.
<EOS>
They
were
those
who
had
come
in
the
previous
afternoon
with
Lord
Byfield
,
and
who
,
having
been
allowed
to
rest

midday
after
their
long
fighting

,
were
now
being
told
off
to
their
various
sections
of
the
defence
line
.
<EOS>
The
Guard
regiments
were
allocated
to
the
northernmost
position
between
Fort
Royston
and
Fort
Skips
.
<EOS>
The

were
to
go
to
Copped
Hall
,
and
the
Seaforths
to
form
the
nucleus
of
a
central

of
Militia
and

,
which
was
being

just
north

.
<EOS>
Epping

and
the
contiguous

were
confided
to
the
Leinster

,
which
alone
of
Sir
Jacob
Stellenbosch
's

had
escaped

,
supported

.
<EOS>
The
field

were
distributed
under

of
the
woods
on
the
south
,
east
,
and
north
-
east
of

.
 
<EOS>
"
During
the
afternoon
the
welcome
news
arrived
that
the
remainder
of
Lord
Byfield
's
command
from
Baldock
,
Royston
,

had
safely

at
Enfield

.
<EOS>
We
may
now
hope
that
what
with
Regulars
,
Militia
,
Volunteers
,
and
the
new
levies
,
our
lines
are
fully

,
and
will

to
stay

of
even

at

.
<EOS>
It

,
from
Brentwood
,
that
great

already

in

and

the
broken

of
the
1st

5th
Armies
that
got

after

.
<EOS>
Victorious

,
the
Germans
must

have
suffered

,
which
may
give

.
"

<EOS>
The

"

"

,

.
<EOS>
It

Garde

,

.
 
<EOS>
From

,

.
<EOS>
Our

the

's

.
<EOS>
The

,

:
 
<EOS>
"
_
Sept
.
15._--So
far
<EOS>
the
bold
strategy
of
our
Commander
-
in
-
Chief
,
in
pushing
the
greater
part
of
the
Xth
Corps
directly
to
the
west
immediately
after
our
victory
at
Chelmsford
,
has
been
amply
justified
by
results
.
<EOS>
Although
we
just
missed
cutting
off
Lord
Byfield
and
a
large
portion
of
his
command
at
Harlow
,
we
gained
a
good
foothold
inside
the
British
defences
north
of
Epping
,
and
I
do
n't
think
it
will
be
long
before
we
have
very
much
improved
our
position
here
.
<EOS>
The
IVth
Corps
arrived
at
Harlow
about
midday
yesterday
in
splendid
condition
,
after
their
long
march
from
Newmarket
,
and
the
residue
of
the
Xth
joined

at

.
<EOS>
As
there
is
nothing
like
keeping
the
enemy
on
the
move
,
no
time
was
lost
in
preparing
to
attack
him
at
the
very

.
<EOS>
As
soon
as
it
was
dark
the
IVth
Corps
got
its
heavy
guns
and
howitzers
into
position
along
the
ridge
above
Epping
Upland
,
and
sent
the
greater

of
its

a

of

at
Skip
's

.
 
<EOS>
"
The
IXth
Corps
,
which
had
arrived
from
Chelmsford
that

,
also
placed
its
field
artillery
in

,
from

its

crossed
that
of
the
IVth
Corps
.
<EOS>
This
Corps
also
provided
the
assaulting
troops
.
<EOS>
The
Xth
Corps
,
which
had
been
engaged
all
day
on
Thursday
,
was
held
in
reserve
.
<EOS>
The
howitzers
on
Epping
Upland
opened
fire
with
petrol
shell
on
the
belt
of
woods
that
lies
immediately
in
rear
of
the
position
to

attacked
,
and
with

wind
succeeded
in
setting

on

and
cutting
off
the
most

section
of
the
British
defences
from

.
<EOS>
This
was
soon
after
midnight
.
<EOS>
The
conflagration
not
only
did
us
this
service
,
but
it
is
supposed
so
attracted
the
attention
of
the
partially
-
trained
soldiers
of
the
enemy
that
they

not

the
IXth
Corps
massing
for

.
 
<EOS>
"
We
then
plastered
their
trenches
with
shrapnel
to
such
an
extent
that
they
did
not
dare
to
show
a
finger
above
them
,
and
finally
carried
the
northern
corner
by

.
<EOS>
To
give
the
enemy
their
due
,
they
fought
well
,
but
we
outnumbered
them
five
to
one
,
and
it
was
impossible
for
them
to
resist
the
onslaught
of
our
well
-

.
<EOS>
News
came
to
-
day
that
the
Saxons
have
been
making

before
Brentwood
with
a

of
keeping

British
employed
down

.
<EOS>
At

they
have
been
steadily

Kelvedon
Hatch
from

Heath
.
 
<EOS>
"
We
hear
,
too
,
that
the
Garde
Corps
have
got
down

,
and
that

from

,

's

,
spread
all

,

Lea
away

westward
,

driven
the
whole
of

under

.
<EOS>
Once
we
succeed
in
rolling
up
the
enemy
's
troops
in
this

,
it
will
not

long
before

London
.
<EOS>
"
 
"
_
Sept
.
16._--Fighting
went
on
all
yesterday
in
the
neighbourhood
of
Skip
's
Corner
.
<EOS>
We
have
taken
the
redoubt
at
North
Weald
Basset
and
driven
the
English
back
into
the
belt
of
burnt
woodland
,
which
they
now
hold
along

.
<EOS>
All

,
too
,
our

guns
,
hidden
away

groves
and
woods
above
Epping
Upland
,
poured
their
heavy
projectiles
on
Epping
and
its

.
<EOS>
We
set
the
village
on
fire
three
times
,
but
the
British
contrived
to
extinguish
the

on
each

.
 
<EOS>
"
I
fancy
Epping
itself
will
be
our
next
point
of
attack
.
<EOS>
"
 
<EOS>
"
_
Sept
.
17._--We
are
still
progressing
,
fighting
is
now
all
but
continuous
.
<EOS>
How
long
it
may
last
I
have
no
idea
.
<EOS>
Probably
there
will
be
no
suspension
of
the
struggle
until
we
are
actually
masters
of
the
Metropolis
.
<EOS>
We
took
advantage
of
the
darkness
to
push
forward
our
men
to
within
three
thousand
yards
of
the
enemy
's
line
,
placing
them
as
far
as
possible
under
cover
of
the
numerous

,
plantations
,
and

which
cover

face
of
this

.
<EOS>
At
4
a.m.
the
General
ordered
his
staff
to
assemble
at
Latton
Park
,
where
he
had
established
his
headquarters
.
<EOS>
He
unfolded
to
us
the
general
outline
of
the
attack
,
which
,
he
now
announced
,
was
to
commence
at
six
precisely
.
 
<EOS>
"
I
thought
myself
that
it
was
a
somewhat
inopportune
time
,
as
we
should
have
the
rising
sun
right
in

;
but
I

that
the
idea
was
to

as

as

us
.
<EOS>
For
although

had
employed
a
night

against
Skip
's

,
and
successfully
too
,
yet
the
general
feeling
in
our
Army
has

of

.
<EOS>
The
possible
gain
is
,
I
think
,
in
no

with
the
probable

of

and

.
<EOS>
The
principal

was
the

of
Epping

;
but

,

,

,

Royston
,

.
<EOS>
The
IXth
Corps
was
to

-

break

English
lining
the

.
<EOS>
It

in

from

.
<EOS>
At

we
mounted
,
and
rode
off
to
Rye
Hill
about

,
from

General

to

.
<EOS>
The
first
rays
of
the
rising
sun
were

with

as

off
,
the
long

ridge
on
which
the
enemy
had
his
position
standing
up

against

.
 
<EOS>
[
Illustration
:
GERMAN
ATTACK
ON
THE
LINES
OF
LONDON
]
 
<EOS>
"
As
we
topped
Rye
Hill
I
could
see
the
thickly
-
massed
lines
of
our

<EOS>
"
We

"
 
"

<EOS>
CHAPTER

.
 
<EOS>
BOMBARDMENT

.
  
<EOS>

.
<EOS>

<EOS>

.
 
<EOS>
Alarming

<EOS>
In

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
CHAPTER

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
Around
Holborn
Circus
immense
damage
was
being
caused
,
and
several

bursting
on
the

itself
blew

bridge
.
 
<EOS>
So

,

,
was
the

,
that

to

the

.
<EOS>
If

,

.
<EOS>
The

the

,
and

.
 
<EOS>
South

<EOS>
very

.
<EOS>
The
German

and
long
-

could

.
<EOS>
One

.
 
<EOS>
When

end
?
<EOS>
Where
would
it
end
?
 
<EOS>
Half

had

,
and

,

.
 
<EOS>
London
was
cowed
.
<EOS>
These

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Every

.
<EOS>
A

,
but

<EOS>
all

.
<EOS>
The

were
at

,

.
 
<EOS>
As

.
<EOS>
And

.
<EOS>
Overhead

the

.
 
<EOS>
Hitherto

,

,
but

Von

.
<EOS>
He

,

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
They

,

,

.
<EOS>
Death

.
 
<EOS>
Dusk

.
<EOS>
Above

buildings
<EOS>
the

was

.
<EOS>
From

,

,

.
<EOS>
Yet

,

.
 
<EOS>
Meanwhile

.
<EOS>
About

.
 
<EOS>
This

,

.
<EOS>
At

,

.
<EOS>
Then

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

.
<EOS>
Hundreds

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
Many

<EOS>
many

.
 
<EOS>
Till

,
and

the

that

still

,
nay
,

.
<EOS>
Then

.
 
<EOS>
Von

's

.
 
<EOS>
All

had
been

.
 
<EOS>
London
lay
burning
--
at
the

.
 
<EOS>
And

-
in
-
Chief

,

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
London

"

!
 
<EOS>
And

,

!
<EOS>
due

--

!
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
VI
.
 
<EOS>
FALL
OF
LONDON
.
  
<EOS>
Outside
London
the
September
night
had
settled
down
on
the
blood
-
stained
field
of
battle
.
<EOS>
With
a
pale
light
the
moon
had
risen
,
partly
hidden
by
chasing
clouds
,
her
white
rays
mingling
with
the
lurid
glare
of
the
fires
down
in
the
great
terrified
Metropolis
below
.
<EOS>
Northward
,
from
Hampstead
across
to
Barnet
--
indeed
,
over
that
wide
district
where
the
final
battle
had
been
so
hotly

--
the

shone
upon
the
pallid

the
fallen
.
 
<EOS>
Along
the
German
line
of
investment
there
had
now
followed
upon
the
roar

battle
an
uncanny

.
 
<EOS>
Away
to
the
west
,
however
,
there
was
still
heard
the
growling
of
distant
conflict
,
now
mounting
into
a
low

of
musketry
fire
,
and
again

away
in

.
<EOS>
The
last
remnant
of
the
British
Army
was
being
hotly

in
the
direction
of
Staines
.
 
<EOS>
London
was
invested
and
bombarded
,
but
not
yet

.
 
<EOS>
For
a
long
time
the
German
Field
Marshal
had
stood
alone
upon
Hampstead
Heath
apart
from
his
staff
,
watching
the
great

of
flame

up
here
<EOS>
and
there

.
<EOS>
His
grey
,

were
contracted
,
his
thin

,
his
hard

,
unable
to
fully

as

.
<EOS>
Von
Kronhelm
's

had
long
ago

.
<EOS>
The
Kaiser
had
likened
him
to
Moltke
,
and
had
declared
that
"
he
could
be
silent
in
seven
languages
.
<EOS>
"
<EOS>
His

was
one
of

,
and
yet
he
was
the
most

of
men
,
and
perhaps

.
<EOS>
Often

he
had

-

,

.
<EOS>
On

actually

,

.
<EOS>
Such

,
who
,

,
were

.
 
<EOS>
Yet

stood

the

.
<EOS>
And

were

about
North
End
,
North

,
South

,
Muswell

,

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
Time

,

.
<EOS>
He

,

.
 
<EOS>
For

,

<EOS>
Then

.
 
<EOS>
This

<EOS>
Bugles

.
<EOS>
Men

,

that

the

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
Still
,
the

had
been

,
and

.
<EOS>
They

expected

,

Kronhelm

.
<EOS>
He

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

.
 
<EOS>
Therefore
,

nine

,

,

.
<EOS>
This

.
 
<EOS>
Twenty

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

"
Advance

,
"

,

.
<EOS>
Major
van

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Londoners

,

.
<EOS>
The

,
No
.
92
,

,

.
 
<EOS>
Five

,

,

.
<EOS>
Every

;

,

,

,
until

,

,

.
<EOS>
Volley

,

,

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
For

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

therefore

these

,
and

house

.
<EOS>
To

.
 
<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
Britons

fought
hand

.
<EOS>
A

.
<EOS>
Quite

,

,

<EOS>
Thus

.
 
<EOS>
This

.
<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
There

.
<EOS>
Londoners

.
 
<EOS>
Against

-

Teutons

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

of

of

,
and

.
 
<EOS>
The

in
the

<EOS>

<EOS>
London

<EOS>
CHAPTER

<EOS>
GERMANS

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

,

,

.
<EOS>
What

?
<EOS>
It

<EOS>

<EOS>
1

<EOS>

<EOS>
2

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
India

<EOS>
5

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
His

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
The

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
What

<EOS>

<EOS>
The
German
Field

sat

.
<EOS>
Had

been

the
true
state
of
affairs
<EOS>
he

.
<EOS>
True
,

Byfield

,

,

,

's
forces
that
shall
either

their

or

.
<EOS>
Englishmen

.
<EOS>
I

,

,

.
<EOS>
Let

.
<EOS>
The

,

is

,
"

.
<EOS>
"

,

.
<EOS>

,

's

.
<EOS>
They

,

,

.
<EOS>
I
will
lead
!
"
<EOS>
he

;
"
who
will
follow
me
?
"
 
<EOS>
In

,

<EOS>
In

<EOS>
In

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Therefore

,

how

.
     
<EOS>
NOTICE
AND
ADVICE
.
     
<EOS>
TO
THE
CITIZENS
OF
LONDON
.
     
<EOS>
I
ADDRESS
YOU
SERIOUSLY
.
     
<EOS>
We
are
neighbours
,
and
in
time
of
peace
cordial
relations
have
    
always
existed
between
us
.
<EOS>
I
therefore
address
you
from
my
heart
    
in
the
cause
of
humanity
.
     
<EOS>
Germany
is
at
war
with
England
.
<EOS>
We
have
been
forced
to
penetrate
    
into
your
country
.
     
<EOS>
But
each
human

spared
,
and
all
property
saved
<EOS>
,
we
regard
as
    
in
the

of
both

and

.
     
<EOS>
We
are
at
war
,
and
both

have
fought

.
     
<EOS>
Our

is
,
however
,
to
spare
disarmed
citizens
and
the
    
inhabitants
of
all

and

.
     
<EOS>
We

a

,
and
we
wish
to
have

known
    
<EOS>
that

any

to

,
    
either

.
     
<EOS>
To

,

,

.
     
<EOS>
I

,

,

,

,

.
     
<EOS>
All

Judge
,

.
     
<EOS>
I

to

,
and
I

.
     
<EOS>
Take
notice
!
     
<EOS>
=
VON
KRONHELM
,
    
Commanding

GERMAN
MILITARY

,
    
WHITEHALL
,

,
_

<EOS>
While

<EOS>
He

.
<EOS>
He

<EOS>
Yet

<EOS>
Even

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
In

<EOS>

<EOS>
While

<EOS>
One

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Speaking

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

,

<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
The

into

more

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
There

<EOS>
Three

<EOS>
Two

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Into

<EOS>
CHAPTER
VIII
.
 
<EOS>
DEFENCES
OF
SOUTH
LONDON
.
  
<EOS>
Preparations
were
being

night

to
place
the
working
-
class

in

and

in

,

.
<EOS>
Everybody
lent

,

.
<EOS>
People

hitherto

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
It

,

,

.
<EOS>
In

from

,

,

were

,

or

.
<EOS>
The
dense

of
South
London
had
already

.
<EOS>
They
were
penned
in
by
the

in

,
for
each

seemed

now

,

.
 
<EOS>
[
Illustration
:

}
]
 
Kennington
,

,

,

"

.
<EOS>
"

"

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
In

--

--

,

.
<EOS>
Thus

.
 
<EOS>
This

<EOS>
Mistakes

.
<EOS>
Day

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

,

,

.
,

.
<EOS>
The

,

now
earth
,

,

,

-

,

.
 
<EOS>
From

all

south

the
river
<EOS>
the

-
stones
were

,

<EOS>
the

.
<EOS>
The

,

.
<EOS>
In

,

,

,

,
Camberwell

,

<EOS>
a

of

's

was

.
<EOS>
They

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
While

.
 
<EOS>
Thus

,

,
"

<EOS>
Contrary

.
<EOS>
He

,

.
<EOS>
Full

,

the
river
,
would
have
no

,
and
would
,

,

to
a
man
.
<EOS>
His

reported
that

rendered
the
populace
the
stronger
,
yet
he
did
nothing
,

,

and

to

that

.
 
<EOS>
Everywhere

Defenders
.
<EOS>
Day

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

of

was
now
upon

's

.
<EOS>
He

,

,

saviour
of

.
<EOS>
Every

.
 
<EOS>
Report

,

--

.
<EOS>
Therefore

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

.
<EOS>
Even

,

,

<EOS>
Some

.
 
<EOS>
The

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

,

a

<EOS>
,

.
 
<EOS>
The

,

,

<EOS>
so

.
<EOS>
The

.
 
<EOS>
The

,

.
 
<EOS>
In
the
Old
Kent
Road
<EOS>
the
bridge
over
the

,

Street
,

,

,

,

,

from

to

forming

.
<EOS>
Clapham

,

.
 
<EOS>
Lord

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
To

,

.
 
<EOS>
Affairs

,

,

-

.
 
<EOS>
Very

's

,
but

.
<EOS>
The

that

.
<EOS>
From

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
Any

.
<EOS>
Therefore
,

,

.
 
<EOS>
From

,

.
<EOS>
In

,

.
<EOS>
Now

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
Yet
,

,

,

.
<EOS>
It

,

.
<EOS>
The

had
,

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

were
across
King

,

,

,

,

,

<EOS>
All

<EOS>
With

.
<EOS>
The

.
<EOS>
The

<EOS>
Crafty

<EOS>
Thus

,

,

<EOS>
At

.
<EOS>
Contrary

,

.
<EOS>
Many

-

<EOS>
Nevertheless

<EOS>
Lord

<EOS>
All

,

<EOS>
The

,

.
 
<EOS>
Yet

,

.
<EOS>
Already

.
<EOS>
The

,

,

,

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

;

<EOS>
German

,

<EOS>
One

<EOS>
Therefore

,

.
 
<EOS>
Accounts

,
but

.
<EOS>
The

,

.
 
<EOS>
Government
,

,

,

.
<EOS>
It

every

--
to

--

.
 
<EOS>
Through

south

of
England

's
clear
,

was
raised
everywhere
,
and
the
whole

were
now
fast

beneath
the

,

in

.
 
<EOS>
The
swift

being
meted

--
or
,

,
to

--

,
he

,

;
but

at

.
<EOS>
Von
Kronhelm
was
the
arch
-

of

,
and

that

.
<EOS>
Londoners
detested

Germans
,
but

did

,

engaged
in
commercial

in
England
,
had
joined
the
colours
and
were
now
acting
as

.
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
IX
.
 
<EOS>
REVOLTS
IN
SHOREDITCH
AND
ISLINGTON
.
  
<EOS>
On
the
night
of
September
27
,
a
very
serious
conflict
,
entailing
much
loss
of
life
on
both
the
London
civilian
and
German
side
,
occurred
at
the
point
where
Kingsland
Road
joins
Old
Street
,
Hackney
Road
,
and
High
Street
.
<EOS>
Across
both
Hackney
and
Kingsland
Roads
the
barricades
built
before
the
bombardment
still
remained
in
a
half
-
ruined
state
,
any
attempt
at
clearing
them
away
being
repulsed
by
the
angry
inhabitants
.
<EOS>
Dalston
,
Kingsland
,
Bethnal
Green
,
and
Shoreditch
were
notably
antagonistic
to
the
invaders
,
and
several
sharp
encounters
had
taken
place
.
<EOS>
Indeed
,
those
districts
were
discovered
by
the
enemy
to
be
very
unsafe
.
 
<EOS>
The
conflict
in
question
,
however
,
commenced
at
the
corner
of
Old

<EOS>

<EOS>
How

<EOS>
We

<EOS>

<EOS>

'

.

_

_

<EOS>
"

,

<EOS>

.
 
<EOS>
"
The

,

<EOS>
Below

"
 
<EOS>
"
To

<EOS>
Londoners

<EOS>
The
"

,

'

<EOS>
"
Soon

<EOS>
"

<EOS>
"

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
"
     
<EOS>
"
_
<EOS>
Later
.
<EOS>
_
 
<EOS>
"
The

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
Lawlessness

<EOS>
"
In

<EOS>
"

<EOS>

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
But

<EOS>
"

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
"
10

<EOS>
"
There

<EOS>
"

<EOS>
"

<EOS>

<EOS>

"

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
"

<EOS>
The

<EOS>
"
At

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
Against

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
"

<EOS>

seemed
as
though
,
for
the
moment
,
the
enemy
had
vanished
.
<EOS>
There
was
only
the
strong
cordon
across
the
bottom
of
Ludgate
Hill
,
men
who
looked
on
in
wonder
,
but

.
 
<EOS>
"
Is

possible
that
Von
Kronhelm
's

to

,
and

to

?
 
<EOS>
"
The

fired
,

,
was

Strand

of
Fleet

,

corner

.
<EOS>
There

,

,

.
<EOS>
Quickly

.
<EOS>
For

the
corner

Aldwych
;

,

along

,

from

Road

into

,
but

.
 
<EOS>
"
The
men

had
for

bridge

more

;

,

in

.
 
<EOS>
"
They
were
not
long
in

.
<EOS>
I
can
only

a
very
brief
_
resume
_
at
the
moment
,
for
they

so

.
 
<EOS>
"
Speaking
generally
,
the
whole
of
London
has
obeyed
the

of
the
League
,
and
,
rising
,
are
attacking
the
Germans
at

.
<EOS>
In
the
majority
of
cases
,
however
,
the
enemy
hold
strong

,
and
are
defending

,

upon

unorganised

.
<EOS>
Every

for

,
without

for
orders

.
<EOS>
In
Bethnal
Green
the

,

the

of
by
-

,
have

great

,
and
again
in
Clerkenwell
,
St.
Luke
's
,
Kingsland
,

.
<EOS>
Whitechapel

,

,
who

Essex
,
has
held

,
and
the
enemy
have
had
some
great

in

.
 
<EOS>
"
With

across
Waterloo
Bridge
,
South

is
,
as

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
"
News

ten

of

upon
the
Saxons
by
Lord
Byfield
from

,
but

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
"
From

office

river
I

fight
,

.
<EOS>
In
Southwark

evidently
most

,
and

.
 
<EOS>
"
There

just

an

so

that
the

this

as
