.
 
<EOS>
"
London
has
struck
her
first
blow
of
revenge
.
<EOS>
What
will
be
its
sequel
?
"
    
<EOS>
CHAPTER
II
.
 
<EOS>
SCENES
AT
WATERLOO
BRIDGE
.
  
<EOS>
The
following
is
the
personal
narrative
of
a
young
chauffeur
named
John
Burgess
,
who
assisted
in
the
defence
of
the
barricade
at
Waterloo
Bridge
.
 
<EOS>
The
statement
was
made
to
a
reporter
at
noon
on
October
5
,
while
he
was
lying
on
a
mattress
in
the
Church
of
St.
Martin's
-
in
-
the
-
Fields
,
so
badly
wounded
in
the
chest
that
the
surgeons
had
given
him
up
.
 
<EOS>
He
related
his
story
in
the
form
of
a
farewell
letter
to
his
sister
.
<EOS>
The
reporter
chanced
to
be
passing
,
and
,
hearing
him
asking
for
some
one
to
write
for
him
,
volunteered
to
do
so
.
 
<EOS>
"
We
all
did
our
best
,
"
he
said
,
"
every
one
of
us
.
<EOS>
Myself
,
I
was
at
the
barricade
for
thirteen
days
--
thirteen
days
of
semi
-
starvation
,
sleeplessness
,
and
constant
tension
,
for
we
knew
not
,
from
one
moment
to
another
,
when
a
sudden
attack
might
be
made
upon
us
.
<EOS>
At
first
our
obstruction
was
a
mere
ill
-
built
pile
of
miscellaneous
articles
,
half
of
which
would
not
stop
bullets
;
but
on
the
third

our
men
,
superintended
by

non
-
commissioned
officers

uniform
,
began
to
put
the
position
in
a

,
to

Maxims
in
the
neighbouring

,
and
to
place
explosives
in
the

of
two
of
the
arches

the
bridge
,
so
that
we
could
instantly

.
 
<EOS>
"
Fully
a
thousand
men
were
holding
the
position
,
but
unfortunately
few
of
them
had
ever
handled
a
rifle
.
<EOS>
As
regards
myself
,
I
had
learned
to
shoot
rooks
when
a
boy
in
Shropshire
,
and
now
that
I
had
obtained
a
gun
I
was
anxious
to
try
my
skill
.
<EOS>
When
the
League
of
Defenders
was
started
,
and
a
local
secretary
came
to
us
,
we
all
eagerly
joined
,
each
receiving
,
after
he
had
taken
his
oath
and
signed
his
name
,
a
small
silk
Union
Jack
,
the
badge
of
the
League
,
not
to
be
worn
till
the
word
went
forth

rise
.
 
<EOS>
"
Then
came
a
period
--
long
,
dreary
,
shadeless
days
of
waiting
--
when
the
sun
beat
down
upon
us
mercilessly
and
our
vigilance
was
required
to
be
constant
both
night
and
day
.
<EOS>
So
uncertain
were
the
movements
of
the
enemy
opposite
us
that
we
scarcely
dared
to
leave
our
positions
for
a
moment
.
<EOS>
Night
after
night
I
spent
sleeping
in
a
neighbouring
doorway
,
with
an
occasional
stretch
upon
somebody
's
bed
in
some
house
in
the
vicinity
.
<EOS>
Now
and
then
,
whenever
we
saw
Germans
moving
in
Wellington
Street
,
we
sent
a
volley
into
them
,
in
return
receiving
a
sharp
reply
from
their

-

.
<EOS>
Constantly
our
sentries
were
on
the
alert
along
the
wharves
,
and
in
the
riverside

,
watching
for
the
approach
of
the
enemy
's
spies
in

.
<EOS>
Almost
nightly
some
adventurous
spirits
among
the
Germans
would
try
and
cross
.
<EOS>
On
one
occasion
,
while
doing
sentry

in
a
warehouse
backing
on
Commercial
Road
,
I
was
sitting
with
a
comrade

a

.
<EOS>
The
moon
was
shining
,
for
the
night
was
a
balmy
and
beautiful
one
,
and
all
was
quiet
.
<EOS>
It
was
about
two
o'clock
in
the
morning
,
and
as

sat

,
with

,
we
suddenly

a
small
boat

men

.
 
<EOS>
"
For
a

the
rowers
rested
upon

,
as

,
then
pulled
forward

in

of
a

-

.
<EOS>
As
they
passed
below

window
I
shouted
a
challenge
.
<EOS>
At
first
there
was
no
response
.
<EOS>
Again
I
repeated
it
,
when
I
heard
a
muttered

in
German
.
 
<EOS>
"
'
Spies
!
'
<EOS>
I
cried
to
my
comrade
,
and
with
one
accord
we
raised
our
rifles
and
fired
.
<EOS>
Ere
the
echo
of
the
first
shot
had
died
away
<EOS>
I
saw
one
man
fall
into
the
water
,
while
at
the
next
shot
a
second
man
half
rose
from
his
seat
,
threw
up
his
hands
,
and
staggered

.
 
<EOS>
"
The
firing
gave
the
alarm
at
the
barricade
,
and
ere
the
boat
could
approach
the
bridge
,
though
the
survivor
pulled
for
dear
life
,
a
Maxim
spat
forth
its
red

,
and
both

and

were

.
 
<EOS>
"
Almost
every
night
similar
incidents
were
reported
.
<EOS>
The
enemy
were
doing
all
in
their
power
to
learn
the
exact
strength
of
our
defences
,
but
I
do
not
think
their
efforts

very

.
<EOS>
The
surface
of
the
river
,
every
inch
of
it
,
was
under
the
careful

.
 
<EOS>
"
Each
day
the
'
Bulletin
'

our
national

.
 
<EOS>
"
At
last
,
however
,
the

on
the
morning

October
4
,
that

ten
that

.
<EOS>
A

bill

,
and
as

the
report

known
we
were
all
highly
excited
,
and
through
the
day

ourselves
for

.
 
<EOS>
"
A
gun
sounded
from
the
direction
of
Westminster
.
<EOS>
We
looked
at
our
watches
,
and
found
it
was
ten
o'clock
.
<EOS>
Our
bugles
sounded
and
we
sprang
to
our
positions
,
as
we
had
done
dozens
,
nay
,

before
.
<EOS>
I
felt
faint
,
for
I
had
only
had
half
a
pint
of
weak
soup

,
for
the
bread

not

round
.
<EOS>
Nevertheless
,
the

we

about
to
strike
the
blow

and
strength
.
<EOS>
Our
officer
shouted
a
brief
word

command
,
and
next
moment
we
opened
a
withering
fire
upon
the
enemy
's

in
Wellington
Street
.
 
<EOS>
"
In
a
moment
a
hundred
rifles
and
several
Maxims
spat

red

at
us
,
but
as
usual

.
<EOS>
Then

artillery
which
Sir
Francis
Bamford
had
sent

three
days
before
got
into
position
,
and
in
a
few

began
hurling
great

the
German
defences
.
 
<EOS>
"
Behind
us
was
a
great
armed

ready

eager

get
at
the
foe
,
a

,

,
irate
Londoners
,

.
<EOS>
From

,
with

anon

,

.
 
<EOS>
"
For

pounded

the

Wellington
Street
with
our

,
Maxims
,

,
until

,

.
<EOS>
Then

seemed
that
all

.
<EOS>
At

surprised

;
but

,
but

.
 
<EOS>
"
We

the

were

.
<EOS>
Into

midst

we
sent

;
but

.
 
<EOS>
"
The

,

,
<EOS>
at

demanded

so

in

.
<EOS>
For

,
for
the

of
General
Greatorex
,
Commander
-
in
-
Chief

League
,
was
that

.
<EOS>
However
,

South
Londoners
became

,
though

knew

into
what

,
as

.
<EOS>
A

opened
in
the

,

two

were
pouring

Waterloo

,

,

<EOS>
With

.
<EOS>
Indeed

many

that

<EOS>
"
The

.
<EOS>
At

--

.
<EOS>
Men

,

.
<EOS>
London
had
risen

,
and

,

.
<EOS>
Soon

,

,

.
<EOS>
The

,

.
<EOS>
The

's

must

severe

,
for
of
the

men
from
Hamburg

Luebeck
holding
their
end
of
Waterloo
Bridge
<EOS>
I

,
even

.
 
<EOS>
"
Our

,

<EOS>
"
We

.

"
    
CHAPTER
IV
.
 
<EOS>
MASSACRE
OF

IN

.
      
<EOS>
"
'
DAILY

'

,
    
"
_

_

,

"
Through

.
<EOS>
They

.
 
<EOS>

<EOS>

<EOS>
"

.
 
<EOS>
"

.
 
<EOS>
"

<EOS>

<EOS>
"

<EOS>
"

<EOS>
CHAPTER
V.
 
<EOS>
HOW
THE
WAR
ENDED
.
  
<EOS>
Days
passed
--
weary
,
waiting
,
anxious
days
.
<EOS>
A
whole
month
went
by
.
<EOS>
What
had
really
happened
at
sea
was
unknown
.
<EOS>
After
the
truce
,
London
very
gradually
began
to
resume
her
normal
life
,
though
the
gaunt
state
of
the
streets
was
indescribably
weird
.
 
<EOS>
Shops
began
to
open
,
and
as
each
day
passed
,
food
became
more
plentiful
and
consequently
less
dear
.
<EOS>
The
truce
meant
the
end
of
the
war
,
therefore
thanksgiving
services
were
held
in
every
town
and
village
throughout
the
country
.
 
<EOS>
There
were
great
prison
-
camps
of
Germans
at
Hounslow
,
Brentwood
,
and
Barnet
,
while
Von
Kronhelm
and
his
chief
officers
were
also
held
as
prisoners
until
some
decision
through
diplomatic
channels
could
be
arrived
at
.
<EOS>
Meanwhile
a
little
business
began
to
be
done
;
thousands
began
to
resume
their
employment
,
bankers
reopened
their
doors
,
and
within
a
week
the
distress
and
suffering
of
the
poor
became
perceptibly
alleviated
.
<EOS>
The
task
of
burying
the
dead
after
the
terrible
massacre
of
the
Germans
in
the
London
streets
had
been
a
stupendous
one
,
but
so
quickly
had
it
been
accomplished
that
an
epidemic
was
happily
averted
.
 
<EOS>
Parliament
moved
back
to
Westminster
,
and
daily
meetings
of
the
Cabinet
were
being
held
in
Downing
Street
.
<EOS>
These
resulted
in
the
resignation
of
the
Ministry
,
and
with
a
fresh
Cabinet
,
in
which
Mr.
Gerald
Graham
,
the
organiser
of
the
Defenders
,
was
given
a
seat
,
a
settlement
was
at
last
arrived
at
.
 
<EOS>
To
further
describe
the
chaotic
state
of
England
occasioned
by
the
terrible
and
bloody
war
would
serve
no
purpose
.
<EOS>
The
loss
and
suffering
which
it
had
caused
the
country
had
been
incalculable
;
statisticians
estimated
that
in
one
month
of
hostilities
it
had
amounted
to
L500,000,000
,
a
part
of
which
represented
money
transferred
from
British
pockets
to
German
,
as
the
enemy
had
carried
off
some
of
the
securities
upon
which
the
German
troops
had
laid
their
hands
in
London
.
 
<EOS>
Let
us
for
a
moment
take
a
retrospective
glance
.
<EOS>
Consols
were
at
50
;
bread
was
still
1_s
.
<EOS>
_
6_d
.
<EOS>
_
per
loaf
;
and
the
ravages
of
the
German
commerce
-
destroyers
had
sent
up
the
cost
of
insurance
on
British
shipping
sky
-
high
.
<EOS>
Money
was
almost
unprocurable
;
except
for
the
manufacture
of
war
material
,
there
was
no
industry
;
and
the
suffering
and
distress
among
the
poor
could
not

.
<EOS>
In
all
directions
men
,

,
and

.
 
<EOS>
The

were
loud
in

for
"
peace
at
any

,
"
and
the
pro
-
German
and
Stop
-
the
-
War
Party
were
equally
vehement
in

a

.
<EOS>
They
found

for
the
enemy
,
and
forgot
the

and

which
the

had

.
 
<EOS>
They
insisted
that
the
working
class
gained
nothing
,
even

the
British
fleet
was
closely

the

,

when

,

were

,

.
<EOS>
The

of

the

was
a

in
the
way
of
the
party
of

,
and

.
 
<EOS>
Socialism
,

"
Thou

,
"

"

,

-
morrow
<EOS>
we

,
"
had

a

taught
to

and

to

.
<EOS>
In
the
hour
of
trial
,
amidst

,
among
the

of
dead
which
marked
the
prolonged
,
bloody
,
and
terrible

on

and

,
the

of
the
nation
quailed
,
and
there
was
really
no
great
leader
to

to
ways

and

.
 
<EOS>
The
wholesale
destruction
of
food
,
and
particularly

wheat
and
meat
,
removed
from
the
world
's
market
a

of

,
and
had

sent

the
cost
of
food
everywhere
,
outside

United
Kingdom
as
well

in
it
.
<EOS>
At
the
same

,
the
attacks
upon
shipping
laden
with
food
increased
the
cost
of
insurance
to
prohibitive
prices
upon
vessels
freighted
for
the
United
Kingdom
.
<EOS>
The
underwriters
after
the
first
few
captures
by
the
enemy
would
not
insure
at
all
except
for
fabulous
rates
.
 
<EOS>
The
withdrawal
of
all
the
larger
British
cruisers
for
the
purpose
of
defeating
the
main
German
fleets
in
the
North
Sea
left
the
commerce
-
destroyers
a
free
hand
,
and
there
was
no
force
to
meet
them
.
<EOS>
The
British
liners
commissioned
as
commerce
-
protectors
were
too
few
and
too
slow
to
be
able
to
hold
their
adversaries
in
check
.
 
<EOS>
Neutral
shipping
was
molested
by
the
German
cruisers
.
 
<EOS>
Whenever
raw
cotton
or
food
of
any
kind
was
discovered
upon
a
neutral
vessel
bound
for
British
ports
,
the
vessel
was
seized
and
sent
into
one
or
other
of
the
German
harbours
on
the
West
Coast
of
Africa
.
 
<EOS>
The
United
Kingdom
,
indeed
,
might
have
been
reduced
to
absolute
starvation
<EOS>
had
it
not
been
for
the
fact
that
the
Canadian
Government
interfered
in
Canada
to
prevent
similar
German
tactics
from
succeeding
,
and
held
the
German
contracts
for
the
cornering
of
Canadian
wheat
,
contrary
to
public
policy
.
 
<EOS>
The
want
of
food
,
the
high
price
of
bread
and
meat
in
England
,
and
the
greatly
increased
cost
of
the
supplies
of
raw
material
sent
up
the
expenditure
upon
poor
relief
to
enormous
figures
.
<EOS>
Millions
of
men
were
out
of
employment
,
and
in
need
of
assistance
.
<EOS>
Mills
and
factories
in
all
directions
had
closed
down
,
either
because
of
the
military
danger
from
the
operations
of
the
German
armies
,
or
because
of
the
want
of
orders
,
or
,
again
,
because
raw
materials
were
not
procurable
.
 
<EOS>
Unfortunately
,
when
the
invasion
began
,
many
rich
foreigners
who
had
lived
in
England
collected
what
portable
property
they
possessed
and
retired
abroad
to
Switzerland
,
Italy
,
and
the
United
States
.
<EOS>
Their
example
was
followed
by
large
numbers
of
British
subjects
who
had
invested
abroad
,
and
now
,
in
the
hour
of
distress
,
were
able
to
place
their
securities
in
a

and
withdraw
them
to
happier

.
 
<EOS>
They
may
justly
be
blamed
for
this
want
of
patriotism
,
but
their
reply
was
that
they
had
been
unjustly
and
mercilessly
taxed
by
men
who
derided
patriotism
,
misused
power
,
and
neglected
the
real
interests
of
the
nation
in
the

to

.
<EOS>
Moreover
,

-

at
3_s
.
<EOS>
_
6_d
.
<EOS>
_
<EOS>
in

,

,

,
while
into

their

.
 
<EOS>
As

this

,
in
London

country
the
number
of
empty

inordinately

,

-

-
do

to

.
<EOS>
The

of
the

had
heaped

was
cruelly

,
since

.
<EOS>
The

,
in

,

.
<EOS>
There

few

,
but

the

,

,

.
 
<EOS>
Under

,

,

.

<EOS>
Peace
was
finally
signed
on
January
13
,
1911
.
<EOS>
The
British
Empire
emerged
from
the
conflict
outwardly
intact
,
but
internally
so
weakened
that
only
the
most
resolute
reforms
accomplished
by
the
ablest
and
boldest
statesmen
,
could
have
restored
it
to
its
old
position
.
 
<EOS>
Germany
,
on
the
other
hand
,
emerged
with
an

21,000
miles
of
European

,
with
an
extended

on
the
North
Sea
,
fronting
the
United
Kingdom
at
Rotterdam
and
the
Texel
,
and
,

was
calculated
,

.
<EOS>
Practically
the
entire

of
the
war
had
been
borne

England
.
 
<EOS>
As
is
always

case
,
the
poor
suffered
most
.
<EOS>
The
Socialists
,
who
had
declared
against
armaments
,
were
faithless

of
those

they
professed
to

.
<EOS>
Their
dream
of
a
golden
age
proved

.
<EOS>
But
the
true
authors
of
England
's

escaped
blame
for
the
moment
,
and
the
Army
and
Navy
were

of

.
 
<EOS>
When

come
,
it
came
too
late
,
and
could
not

utilised
without
a
great
British
Army
capable
of
carrying
the
war
into
the
enemy
's

,
and
thus
compelling

.
  
<EOS>
THE
END
.
